Research on the use of Scratch in educational settings has shown that the ability to manipulate visual and audio elements is a key factor in fostering creativity and artistic expression [5].

Furthermore, Scratch provides opportunities for students to explore advanced concepts in programming while maintaining a creative and expressive approach. For example, extensions such as DSAScratch and CryptoScratch allow students to experiment with more complex computational concepts, such as data structures and cryptographic algorithms, while still engaging in creative project development. These tools enable students to bridge the gap between creativity and technical proficiency, allowing them to create sophisticated projects that reflect both their artistic vision and their understanding of computational thinking.

The role of creativity in Scratch is also supported by research on the impact of gender and diversity in programming. Studies have shown that Scratch provides an inclusive environment where students of all backgrounds can explore their creativity and develop their programming skills. For instance, research on gender differences in Scratch programs has revealed that girls and boys tend to prefer different types of projects, with girls often focusing on animations and stories, while boys tend to create games with more complex control structures. This diversity in creative expression highlights the adaptability of Scratch in meeting the needs of different learners and promoting inclusive and equitable learning environments [21].

In addition to fostering creativity in individual projects, Scratch also supports collaborative and group-based creative activities. The platform's features, such as the ability to remix and build upon existing projects, encourage students to work together and share their ideas. This collaborative approach not only enhances creativity but also promotes teamwork, communication, and problem-solving skills. Research on the impact of remixing in the Scratch community has shown that collaborative projects can lead to increased creativity and innovation, as students are exposed to a wider range of ideas and perspectives [22].

Overall, Scratch plays a significant role in fostering creativity and artistic expression among learners. By providing a flexible, engaging, and supportive environment, the platform empowers students to explore their creativity, experiment with new ideas, and develop their unique projects. The integration of visual elements, multimedia, and community features further enhances the creative potential of Scratch, making it an invaluable tool for computational thinking education. As research continues to explore the ways in which Scratch can be used to measure and enhance creativity, it is clear that the platform will remain a vital resource for promoting creativity and artistic expression in the digital age.

### 4.7 Collaboration and Social Learning

Scratch has emerged as a powerful tool for fostering collaboration and social learning, enabling students to engage in activities that promote teamwork, communication, and shared problem-solving. One of the key ways Scratch facilitates collaboration is through pair programming, a practice that encourages students to work together on programming tasks. This approach not only helps in building technical skills but also enhances interpersonal communication and collaborative problem-solving abilities. Studies have shown that pair programming can lead to better learning outcomes and increased engagement, as students are encouraged to discuss and refine their ideas in real-time. For instance, research on pair programming in Scratch highlights that students who engage in this practice demonstrate a deeper understanding of programming concepts and are more likely to persist in challenging tasks [34].

In addition to pair programming, Scratch provides a vibrant online community that plays a crucial role in social learning. The Scratch website hosts a vast collection of user-generated projects, allowing students to share their work, receive feedback, and collaborate with peers from around the world. This community aspect fosters a sense of belonging and encourages students to learn from each other's experiences. The platform's features, such as commenting and liking projects, create an environment where students can engage in meaningful discussions about their work, leading to a richer learning experience. Research on the Scratch online community reveals that the collaborative nature of the platform can significantly enhance students' motivation and interest in programming [26].

Group projects are another essential component of collaboration and social learning in Scratch. By working on group projects, students are required to divide tasks, communicate effectively, and combine their individual strengths to achieve a common goal. This collaborative process not only develops their technical skills but also enhances their ability to work in teams, a critical skill in today's workforce. Studies have shown that group projects in Scratch can lead to increased creativity and innovation, as students are exposed to diverse perspectives and ideas. For example, a study on the use of Scratch in K-12 education found that group projects significantly improved students' problem-solving skills and their ability to think critically about their work [88].

Moreover, Scratch's emphasis on creativity and self-expression further enhances collaboration and social learning. Students are encouraged to create projects that reflect their personal interests and ideas, which can be shared and discussed within the community. This creative freedom not only makes learning more engaging but also promotes a sense of ownership and pride in their work. Research on creativity in Scratch highlights that the platform's visual and interactive nature allows students to explore and express their ideas in unique ways, fostering a collaborative environment where students can learn from each other's creativity [5].

The integration of collaborative tools and features within Scratch further supports social learning. For instance, the ability to comment on and remix projects encourages students to build on each other's work, fostering a culture of collaboration and continuous improvement. This aspect of Scratch is particularly valuable as it mirrors real-world collaborative practices, preparing students for future academic and professional environments. A study on collaborative learning in Scratch found that the platform's features significantly enhanced students' ability to work together, leading to improved learning outcomes and a more positive attitude towards programming [10].

In addition to these collaborative features, Scratch's role in promoting social learning is further emphasized by its ability to support diverse learning styles and needs. The platform's flexibility allows students to engage in collaborative activities at their own pace and in ways that suit their individual learning preferences. This inclusivity ensures that all students, regardless of their background or skill level, can participate in and benefit from collaborative learning experiences. Research on inclusive education with Scratch highlights that the platform's adaptability makes it an effective tool for supporting diverse learners, including those with special needs [13].

Furthermore, the use of Scratch in after-school programs and informal learning environments has been shown to enhance collaboration and social learning. These programs often provide a more relaxed and supportive environment where students can engage in collaborative projects and activities without the constraints of a traditional classroom. This setting encourages students to take risks, explore new ideas, and learn from their peers. A study on afterschool programs using Scratch found that the collaborative nature of the activities significantly improved students' engagement and interest in programming [27].

The impact of collaboration and social learning in Scratch is also evident in the way it supports the development of communication skills. As students work together on projects, they are required to articulate their ideas, listen to others, and negotiate solutions. This process not only enhances their technical skills but also improves their ability to communicate effectively, a vital skill in both academic and professional settings. Research on the role of collaboration in Scratch highlights that the platform's features effectively support the development of communication skills, leading to more effective teamwork and problem-solving [70].

In conclusion, Scratch plays a significant role in facilitating collaboration and social learning, offering a range of features and opportunities that support teamwork, communication, and shared problem-solving. Through pair programming, online communities, group projects, and collaborative tools, Scratch creates an engaging and inclusive environment where students can learn from each other and develop essential skills. The platform's emphasis on creativity and self-expression further enhances collaboration, encouraging students to explore and share their ideas. As research continues to highlight the benefits of collaborative learning in Scratch, it becomes clear that the platform is a valuable tool for fostering social learning and preparing students for future academic and professional challenges.

### 4.8 Integration with Emerging Technologies

The integration of emerging technologies into Scratch-based learning represents a significant evolution in computational thinking education, offering new possibilities for enhancing student engagement, creativity, and problem-solving skills. Emerging technologies such as artificial intelligence (AI), machine learning, and blockchain are being increasingly incorporated into Scratch-based learning environments to provide more advanced and interactive learning experiences. Tools like CryptoScratch and AI-augmented systems such as ChatScratch are at the forefront of this innovation, enabling learners to explore complex computational concepts in a more accessible and engaging manner.

One of the most notable examples of integrating emerging technologies with Scratch is the development of CryptoScratch, a block-based programming tool that incorporates modern cryptographic algorithms into the Scratch environment. CryptoScratch allows K-12 students to learn about fundamental concepts such as confidentiality, authentication, and integrity protection through visual blocks. This tool enables students to implement cryptographic algorithms like AES, RSA, and SHA-256, making it possible for them to explore and understand complex topics in cybersecurity and data protection. By using a block-based interface, CryptoScratch simplifies the process of learning and applying cryptographic principles, making it more accessible for young learners [32]. This integration of blockchain and cryptographic concepts into Scratch-based learning not only enhances students' understanding of computational thinking but also prepares them for the technological challenges of the future.

In addition to blockchain, AI is playing a crucial role in transforming Scratch-based learning. AI-augmented systems like ChatScratch are designed to provide personalized and autonomous learning experiences for young children aged 6-12. ChatScratch employs structured interactive storyboards and visual cues to overcome artist’s block in project planning, integrates digital drawing and advanced image generation technologies to elevate creativity, and leverages Scratch-specialized Large Language Models (LLMs) for professional coding guidance. This AI-powered system not only supports students in creating high-quality and personally meaningful Scratch projects but also enhances their ability to learn programming independently. ChatScratch’s ability to provide intelligent guidance and real-time feedback makes it a valuable tool for fostering computational thinking and creativity in young learners [19].

Another important area of integration is the use of machine learning in Scratch-based learning environments. Machine learning algorithms can be used to analyze student behavior, provide personalized feedback, and improve the overall learning experience. For example, automated testing frameworks such as Whisker and tools like Catnip have been developed to generate next-step hints for students who are stuck while solving programming tasks. These systems use machine learning to analyze student code and generate hints that help them improve their programs. Catnip, in particular, extends existing data-driven hint generation approaches by incorporating automated testing, which allows for more accurate and individualized feedback. This integration of machine learning into Scratch-based learning environments not only supports students in overcoming common programming challenges but also promotes a deeper understanding of computational thinking [89].

Moreover, the integration of AI and machine learning into Scratch-based learning is also being explored through the development of adaptive learning systems. These systems use AI to tailor the learning experience to individual student needs, abilities, and learning styles, thereby optimizing learning outcomes. For instance, AI-driven interfaces in intelligent tutoring systems (ITS) have been shown to improve student engagement and motivation by providing personalized feedback and adaptive learning paths. AI-powered interfaces can also help students develop a more profound understanding of computational concepts by offering real-time assistance and interactive learning experiences [90].

The integration of emerging technologies into Scratch-based learning also extends to the use of gamification and interactive learning techniques. Gamification elements, such as challenges, rewards, and interactive exercises, can significantly enhance student motivation and engagement in computational thinking education. For example, tools like ChatScratch and AI-augmented systems leverage gamification to create an immersive and engaging learning environment for young learners. These systems incorporate game-like elements that encourage students to explore and experiment with programming concepts, making the learning process more enjoyable and effective [19].

Additionally, the integration of emerging technologies into Scratch-based learning has the potential to address the challenges of scalability and accessibility in computational thinking education. AI and machine learning can be used to develop scalable and adaptive learning systems that can accommodate a large number of students while maintaining personalized learning experiences. For example, AI-powered platforms can automatically generate assessments, provide real-time feedback, and adapt to the learning needs of individual students. This not only makes computational thinking education more accessible to a wider audience but also ensures that students receive the support they need to succeed [91].

In conclusion, the integration of emerging technologies such as AI, machine learning, and blockchain into Scratch-based learning is transforming the way students engage with computational thinking. Tools like CryptoScratch and ChatScratch are leading the way by providing innovative and interactive learning experiences that enhance student creativity, problem-solving skills, and understanding of complex computational concepts. These advancements not only make computational thinking education more accessible and engaging for young learners but also prepare them for the technological challenges of the future. As emerging technologies continue to evolve, their integration into Scratch-based learning will play a crucial role in shaping the next generation of computational thinkers.

### 4.9 Assessment and Feedback Mechanisms

Assessment and feedback mechanisms are critical components in the educational application of Scratch, as they help students improve their understanding of computational thinking and programming concepts. Automated assessment tools have been developed to provide feedback, track student progress, and enhance learning outcomes in Scratch-based programming. These tools support both instructors and students by offering real-time insights, identifying errors, and suggesting improvements. One such tool is LitterBox, a static analysis tool designed to help teachers and students evaluate and improve their Scratch programs. LitterBox detects common programming errors and offers hints to students, enabling them to understand and correct their mistakes [18]. This tool is particularly valuable in teacher training, as it helps educators create programming tasks that are more effective and aligned with learning objectives.

Another automated assessment tool is Code Perfumes, which is used to evaluate the quality of Scratch programs. Code Perfumes analyzes code for issues such as inefficiency, redundancy, and poor structure, providing feedback to students that can help them refine their projects. This tool is especially useful for identifying patterns that may hinder the development of good programming habits. Research has shown that when students use Code Perfumes, they are more likely to produce clean, well-structured code, which is an essential aspect of computational thinking [18]. By incorporating such tools into the learning process, educators can ensure that students not only learn to code but also develop good coding practices.

Model-based testing is another significant approach to assessment in Scratch-based programming. Model-based testing involves creating a model of the expected behavior of a program and using it to generate test cases. This approach is particularly useful for Scratch, as it allows for the automated generation of tests that can check for functional correctness and reliability. The Whisker framework, for example, is a test generation tool that has been adapted for Scratch programs. Whisker uses model-based testing to ensure that programs behave as intended, making it easier for students to debug and improve their code [92]. By providing automated testing capabilities, model-based testing helps students understand the importance of testing and validation in the software development process.

In addition to these tools, there are also approaches that use search-based test generation to improve the effectiveness of automated testing in Scratch. Search-based test generation involves using algorithms to explore the space of possible test cases and find those that are most likely to reveal bugs or errors in the program. This approach has been shown to be particularly effective for Scratch programs, which often involve randomization and interactive elements that make traditional testing approaches less effective [37]. By leveraging search-based test generation, educators can ensure that students' programs are thoroughly tested and that any issues are identified and resolved early in the development process.

Another important aspect of assessment and feedback in Scratch is the use of automated hint generation. Tools like Catnip have been developed to provide students with next-step hints when they are stuck on a programming task. Catnip uses automated testing to select suitable candidate solutions and generates hints that guide students toward functional improvements in their code. This approach is particularly useful for novice programmers, who may struggle to identify the next steps in their problem-solving process. Studies have shown that the use of automated hints can significantly improve students' ability to complete programming tasks and develop their problem-solving skills [89]. By providing targeted feedback, these tools help students build confidence and improve their understanding of programming concepts.

The integration of AI and machine learning into assessment and feedback mechanisms has also gained traction in Scratch-based learning. AI-powered tools like ChatScratch and CodeHelp provide personalized feedback and assistance to students, making the learning process more engaging and effective. ChatScratch, for instance, uses structured interactive storyboards and visual cues to help students overcome artist's block and create more meaningful Scratch projects [19]. Similarly, CodeHelp provides on-demand assistance to programming students without revealing direct solutions, encouraging them to think critically and develop their problem-solving skills [93].

Moreover, the use of learning analytics has become an essential part of assessment in Scratch-based programming. Learning analytics involves the collection and analysis of data to understand student learning processes and improve educational outcomes. Tools like ClickTree use clickstream data to predict student performance and identify areas where students may need additional support [94]. By analyzing student interactions with Scratch projects, educators can gain insights into how students are engaging with the material and make data-driven decisions to enhance learning.

In summary, assessment and feedback mechanisms play a crucial role in the educational application of Scratch. Automated tools such as LitterBox, Code Perfumes, and model-based testing provide valuable feedback to students, helping them improve their programming skills and develop good coding practices. Search-based test generation and automated hint generation further enhance the learning experience by offering targeted support and guidance. The integration of AI and machine learning into these tools has also shown promise in making the learning process more personalized and effective. By leveraging these assessment and feedback mechanisms, educators can ensure that students not only learn to code but also develop the critical thinking and problem-solving skills necessary for success in computational thinking.

### 4.10 Teacher Training and Support

Teacher training and support are critical components in the effective integration of Scratch into classroom settings. As Scratch has become a widely adopted platform for teaching computational thinking (CT) across various educational levels, the importance of equipping educators with the necessary skills, resources, and pedagogical strategies cannot be overstated. While Scratch offers an intuitive, block-based interface that makes programming accessible to learners of all ages, the successful implementation of Scratch in the classroom depends on the educators’ ability to guide students effectively. This requires not only a thorough understanding of the platform’s features but also an appreciation of the broader educational goals associated with CT. Without proper training and support, educators may struggle to harness the full potential of Scratch, leading to suboptimal learning outcomes for students.

One of the key challenges in teacher training for Scratch is the need to bridge the gap between technical proficiency and pedagogical expertise. Educators must not only become comfortable with the Scratch interface and its capabilities but also develop strategies for integrating it into the broader curriculum. This involves understanding how to design activities that align with CT principles such as decomposition, pattern recognition, abstraction, and algorithm design [2]. For instance, while some teachers may be adept at using Scratch for basic programming tasks, they may lack the knowledge of how to structure lessons that promote higher-order thinking and problem-solving skills. As a result, the potential of Scratch as a tool for fostering deep computational thinking may remain underutilized. 

To address these challenges, several tools and resources have been developed to support educators in their training and professional development. One such tool is LitterBox, a platform designed to help teachers analyze and evaluate student projects in Scratch. LitterBox provides insights into the code structure, logic, and creativity of student projects, enabling teachers to offer targeted feedback and support. By using LitterBox, educators can gain a better understanding of students’ learning processes and identify areas where additional instruction or guidance may be needed [95]. This tool not only enhances the assessment process but also helps teachers refine their pedagogical approaches by providing data-driven insights into student performance.

In addition to tools like LitterBox, the need for comprehensive curriculum development and pedagogical strategies cannot be overlooked. Effective integration of Scratch into the classroom requires well-structured lesson plans that align with educational standards and learning objectives. This includes designing activities that encourage collaboration, creativity, and critical thinking, all of which are essential components of computational thinking [2]. For example, educators can incorporate project-based learning, where students work in groups to create interactive stories, games, or animations, thereby applying CT concepts in meaningful ways. Such approaches not only enhance student engagement but also provide opportunities for peer learning and social interaction, which are vital for developing computational thinking skills.

Moreover, the development of pedagogical strategies that support diverse learning needs is essential. Not all students learn in the same way, and educators must be equipped with the flexibility to adapt their teaching methods to accommodate different learning styles and abilities. This may involve using differentiated instruction, where teachers tailor their lessons to meet the unique needs of individual students. For instance, some students may benefit from visual aids and step-by-step guidance, while others may thrive with more open-ended, exploratory tasks. By leveraging the features of Scratch, such as its visual interface and drag-and-drop blocks, educators can create a more inclusive and accessible learning environment [29].

Another crucial aspect of teacher training is the importance of ongoing professional development. The field of computational thinking and educational technology is constantly evolving, and educators must stay informed about the latest developments and best practices. This includes attending workshops, participating in online courses, and engaging with professional learning communities. Such opportunities not only enhance teachers’ knowledge and skills but also provide a platform for sharing experiences, challenges, and successes. For instance, the MIT RAICA (Responsible AI for Computational Action) curriculum offers modules that help teachers integrate AI and computational thinking into their classrooms, emphasizing the importance of ethical considerations and responsible use of technology [96]. By participating in such programs, educators can gain valuable insights and resources that support their teaching practice.

In addition to formal training programs, the role of peer support and collaboration among educators is also significant. Teachers often benefit from exchanging ideas, strategies, and resources with their colleagues, which can enhance their teaching effectiveness and confidence. This can be facilitated through professional learning communities, where educators can discuss challenges, share successful practices, and collaborate on lesson planning. For example, online platforms such as the Scratch website offer forums and communities where teachers can connect with other educators, share their experiences, and access a wealth of resources and materials. This collaborative approach not only fosters a sense of community but also promotes continuous learning and improvement among educators.

Finally, the need for institutional support and policy development cannot be ignored. Schools and educational institutions must recognize the importance of teacher training and support in the effective use of Scratch and other computational thinking tools. This includes allocating resources for professional development, providing access to necessary technology and software, and creating a supportive environment that encourages innovation and experimentation. By investing in teacher training and support, educational institutions can ensure that Scratch is used to its full potential, benefiting both students and educators alike.

In conclusion, the successful integration of Scratch into the classroom requires a multifaceted approach that includes robust teacher training, support, and professional development. Tools like LitterBox and comprehensive curriculum development are essential for helping educators navigate the complexities of teaching computational thinking. Moreover, the importance of pedagogical strategies, ongoing learning, and institutional support cannot be overstated. By addressing these key areas, educators can effectively harness the power of Scratch to foster computational thinking and prepare students for a future where digital literacy and problem-solving skills are increasingly essential.

## 5 Empirical Studies on Scratch-Based Learning

### 5.1 Overview of Empirical Research on Scratch-Based Learning

The empirical research on Scratch-based learning has significantly contributed to understanding the effectiveness of Scratch as a tool for computational thinking (CT) education. A substantial body of studies has investigated how Scratch impacts various aspects of learning, including problem-solving, creativity, collaboration, and student engagement. These studies have been conducted across diverse educational settings, from primary school to higher education, and have revealed several key themes and research trends that highlight the value of Scratch in promoting computational thinking.

One of the central themes in the empirical studies is the role of Scratch in fostering problem-solving and algorithmic thinking. Researchers have consistently found that Scratch provides an accessible platform for students to develop these critical skills. For example, a study conducted by [4] highlighted that Scratch's visual interface allows students to focus on the logical structure of their programs without being overwhelmed by syntax. This supports the development of computational thinking by enabling learners to engage in activities such as decomposition, pattern recognition, and abstraction. Similarly, [2] emphasized that Scratch is particularly effective in introducing CT concepts to young learners, as it aligns with their cognitive development stages.

Another prominent theme in the literature is the impact of Scratch on creativity and expression. Many studies have explored how Scratch encourages students to create games, animations, and interactive stories, thereby enhancing their creative abilities. For instance, [2] noted that Scratch's block-based programming environment allows students to experiment with different ideas and express their thoughts in a visual and interactive manner. This aligns with findings from [4], which argued that Scratch's flexibility supports a wide range of creative projects, fostering both technical and artistic skills. Furthermore, [97] highlighted that Scratch can be used to develop creative problem-solving skills, as it encourages students to think outside the box and explore multiple solutions to a given problem.

Collaboration and social learning have also emerged as significant themes in the empirical studies on Scratch-based learning. Research has shown that Scratch's online community and project-sharing features promote collaboration among students. For example, [68] found that collaborative projects using Scratch helped students develop teamwork and communication skills. Similarly, [98] discussed how collaborative learning in Scratch environments enhances student engagement and facilitates deeper learning. These studies underscore the importance of social interaction in computational thinking education and highlight how Scratch can serve as a platform for collaborative problem-solving and knowledge sharing.

A growing trend in the empirical research on Scratch-based learning is the integration of emerging technologies to enhance the learning experience. Studies such as [99] have explored the potential of combining Scratch with artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) to provide personalized learning experiences. For instance, [99] discussed the use of AI-driven tools to support students in developing computational thinking skills, emphasizing the role of adaptive learning systems in tailoring instruction to individual needs. Similarly, [100] explored how AI-augmented tools like ChatScratch can enhance Scratch-based learning by providing intelligent guidance and support. These studies indicate a shift towards more interactive and adaptive learning environments, where Scratch serves as a foundation for integrating advanced technologies.

Another key trend is the focus on assessing the effectiveness of Scratch in computational thinking education. Researchers have developed various tools and frameworks to evaluate student learning outcomes and computational thinking skills. For example, [33] introduced the ACE test, which assesses higher-order thinking skills such as analyzing, evaluating, and creating. This test was designed to measure students' ability to apply computational thinking concepts in real-world scenarios, providing valuable insights into the effectiveness of Scratch-based learning. Additionally, [101] highlighted the importance of automated assessment tools in providing timely and relevant feedback to students, enhancing their learning experience.

The empirical studies on Scratch-based learning also emphasize the importance of teacher support and professional development. Research has shown that effective integration of Scratch into the curriculum requires teachers to have the necessary knowledge and skills. [68] and [2] both underscore the need for teacher training programs that provide educators with the resources and strategies to effectively use Scratch in the classroom. These studies suggest that teacher support is crucial in ensuring that Scratch is used to its full potential, as it enables educators to guide students through the learning process and provide meaningful feedback.

Overall, the empirical research on Scratch-based learning reveals a rich and diverse landscape of studies that highlight the effectiveness of Scratch in promoting computational thinking. The key themes and research trends identified in these studies underscore the importance of Scratch as a tool for fostering problem-solving, creativity, collaboration, and personalized learning. As the field continues to evolve, future research will likely explore new ways to integrate emerging technologies into Scratch-based learning, further enhancing its impact on computational thinking education. The insights gained from these studies provide a solid foundation for educators and researchers to continue developing innovative approaches to computational thinking education.

### 5.2 Student Engagement in Scratch-Based Learning

Student engagement is a critical factor in the success of any educational intervention, and Scratch-based learning has shown promising results in this regard. Scratch, a block-based visual programming environment, has been widely used in K-12 education to introduce students to computational thinking (CT) and programming concepts. Studies have consistently shown that Scratch-based learning can significantly enhance student engagement, not only in terms of participation and motivation but also in fostering interest in programming and CT. This subsection explores the impact of Scratch-based learning on student engagement, drawing on empirical research that measures participation, motivation, and interest in programming and CT.

One of the most significant findings in the literature is that Scratch-based learning fosters high levels of student participation. For example, a study conducted in a primary school setting revealed that students who engaged with Scratch-based activities showed increased involvement in classroom tasks compared to those in traditional programming environments [5]. This is likely due to the interactive and visually appealing nature of Scratch, which allows students to create and manipulate programs in a way that is both intuitive and enjoyable. The use of drag-and-drop blocks, which eliminate the need for syntax knowledge, reduces the cognitive load on students, enabling them to focus on problem-solving and creative expression rather than debugging code. This makes the learning process more accessible and engaging, particularly for younger students who may lack prior programming experience.

Motivation is another key aspect of student engagement that has been positively influenced by Scratch-based learning. Research has shown that students are more motivated to engage with Scratch-based activities because they are allowed to express their creativity and take ownership of their learning. For instance, a study conducted with high school students in Chile found that participation in a C++ programming workshop led to significant improvements in CT skills, but the study also noted that students with lower initial CT levels were more likely to drop out [77]. While this study focused on a text-based programming language, it highlights the importance of creating learning environments that are both challenging and engaging. Scratch addresses this by offering a more accessible and interactive platform that allows students to experiment and explore programming concepts without the immediate pressure of syntax errors.

Interest in programming and CT is another important dimension of student engagement that has been positively impacted by Scratch-based learning. A study by [5] found that students who used Scratch to create interactive projects were more likely to express interest in programming and CT. This is because Scratch provides a platform where students can create games, animations, and other interactive media, which makes programming feel more relevant and meaningful. The ability to see immediate results from their efforts further reinforces students' interest and motivation to continue learning. For example, a study on the use of Scratch in a middle school setting found that students were highly engaged in the programming process, as they could see their creations come to life in real-time [4]. This immediate feedback loop not only enhances engagement but also helps students develop a deeper understanding of programming concepts.

In addition to participation, motivation, and interest, several studies have also examined how Scratch-based learning affects students' perceptions of programming and CT. For instance, a study on the use of Scratch in a K-12 setting found that students who used Scratch were more likely to view programming as a creative and enjoyable activity rather than a technical and rigid process [4]. This shift in perception is crucial because it can help reduce the intimidation factor associated with programming, especially for students who may not have considered it a viable career path. The study also noted that students who used Scratch were more likely to express a desire to continue exploring programming concepts beyond the classroom, suggesting that Scratch-based learning can serve as a gateway to more advanced programming and CT activities.

Furthermore, the impact of Scratch on student engagement is not limited to traditional classroom settings. Research has shown that Scratch is also effective in informal learning environments, such as after-school programs and coding clubs. A study on the use of Scratch in an informal learning context found that students were highly engaged in the programming activities, as they had the freedom to explore and experiment with the tool in a more relaxed and self-directed manner [37]. This flexibility is particularly beneficial for students who may not have had the opportunity to engage with programming in a formal educational setting. The study also found that students in informal settings were more likely to collaborate with peers, which further enhanced their engagement and learning outcomes.

Another important factor in student engagement is the role of teacher support and guidance. A study on the use of Scratch in a classroom setting found that teacher involvement played a critical role in maintaining student engagement and ensuring that students remained motivated throughout the learning process [37]. Teachers who provided structured guidance, encouraged collaboration, and offered timely feedback were more likely to see higher levels of student engagement. This suggests that while Scratch is an effective tool for fostering engagement, its success depends on how it is implemented and supported in the classroom.

In conclusion, the empirical evidence strongly supports the notion that Scratch-based learning has a positive impact on student engagement. Studies have shown that Scratch enhances participation, motivation, and interest in programming and CT, making it an effective tool for introducing students to computational thinking. The interactive and visually engaging nature of Scratch allows students to explore programming concepts in a way that is both accessible and enjoyable, fostering a deeper understanding of CT and a greater interest in programming. Moreover, the flexibility of Scratch makes it suitable for both formal and informal learning environments, allowing students to engage with programming in a way that suits their individual learning styles and interests. As more research continues to explore the potential of Scratch in education, it is clear that it plays a vital role in promoting student engagement and preparing the next generation of learners for a technology-driven world.

### 5.3 Learning Outcomes and Computational Thinking Development

The effectiveness of Scratch in improving students' computational thinking (CT) skills has been extensively studied, with empirical research highlighting its significant impact on key areas such as problem-solving, algorithmic thinking, and abstraction. These studies provide compelling evidence that Scratch not only introduces young learners to programming concepts but also fosters the development of critical CT skills that are essential for success in the digital age. 

One of the most notable findings from empirical studies is the positive impact of Scratch on problem-solving abilities. Research by [12] suggests that Scratch enables students to break down complex problems into smaller, manageable parts, a core principle of computational thinking. This is achieved through the block-based interface, which allows learners to experiment with different solutions without the burden of syntax errors. For instance, studies have shown that students who use Scratch are more likely to develop effective strategies for tackling open-ended tasks, as the visual nature of the platform encourages iterative problem-solving and testing of ideas. This aligns with the findings of [21], which highlights how Scratch fosters a creative and exploratory approach to problem-solving, particularly among younger students.

In addition to problem-solving, empirical studies have demonstrated that Scratch significantly enhances algorithmic thinking. Algorithmic thinking involves the ability to design and implement step-by-step procedures to solve problems, and Scratch's block-based environment makes this concept more accessible to beginners. Research by [24] and [37] shows that Scratch helps students understand the logical flow of programs, as they can see the immediate effects of their code. This hands-on experience with algorithms is crucial for developing the ability to think in terms of sequences, loops, and conditional statements—foundational elements of algorithmic thinking. Furthermore, studies such as [48] indicate that Scratch provides a platform for students to explore and refine their understanding of algorithmic structures, leading to improved code quality and more efficient problem-solving approaches.

Another key area where Scratch has shown effectiveness is in the development of abstraction skills. Abstraction is a fundamental CT skill that involves identifying patterns and simplifying complex systems. Empirical studies have shown that Scratch enables students to abstract away the technical details of programming, allowing them to focus on the underlying logic and structure of their projects. For example, [10] demonstrates how Scratch can be used to teach students to analyze and visualize data, promoting the ability to identify patterns and generalize solutions. This is particularly relevant in the context of data science, where abstraction plays a critical role in interpreting and making sense of large datasets. Moreover, the visual and interactive nature of Scratch allows students to engage with abstract concepts in a more tangible and meaningful way, making the learning process more intuitive and effective.

The impact of Scratch on CT development is not limited to individual learning outcomes; it also extends to collaborative and social learning environments. Research by [10] highlights how Scratch promotes teamwork and peer interaction, as students often work together on projects and share their creations with others. This collaborative approach fosters the development of CT skills through discussion, reflection, and the exchange of ideas. Additionally, the [102] provides a platform for learners to engage with a global network of peers, further enhancing their learning experiences. Studies such as [16] show that the social aspect of Scratch contributes to a more dynamic and engaging learning environment, where students can draw inspiration from others and refine their CT skills through continuous interaction.

Empirical studies have also explored the long-term impact of Scratch on students' CT development. For instance, [2] highlights the growing recognition of CT as a fundamental skill that should be integrated into educational curricula at all levels. The study emphasizes the importance of tools like Scratch in bridging the gap between theoretical concepts and practical application, enabling students to develop a deeper understanding of computational thinking. Furthermore, [77] provides evidence that Scratch-based learning can be effective in fostering CT skills, even in more advanced educational settings. The study found that high school students who participated in a C++ programming workshop showed significant improvements in their CT abilities, suggesting that the foundational skills developed through Scratch can be transferred to more complex programming environments.

In conclusion, the empirical studies on Scratch-based learning consistently demonstrate its effectiveness in improving students' computational thinking skills. From problem-solving and algorithmic thinking to abstraction and collaboration, Scratch provides a supportive and engaging environment that fosters the development of CT. The findings underscore the importance of integrating Scratch into educational curricula to equip students with the skills they need to succeed in an increasingly digital world. As research continues to evolve, it is clear that Scratch remains a powerful tool for promoting computational thinking and preparing the next generation of learners for the challenges of the future.

### 5.4 Pedagogical Approaches and Teaching Strategies

Pedagogical approaches and teaching strategies in Scratch-based learning environments have been widely studied and evaluated in empirical research. These studies highlight various methods such as collaborative learning, project-based learning, and guided instruction, each offering unique benefits for fostering computational thinking (CT) in students. Research has shown that these approaches not only enhance student engagement but also improve their problem-solving abilities and understanding of computational concepts.

Collaborative learning is a prominent pedagogical approach in Scratch-based education. Studies have found that when students work in groups, they engage in more meaningful discussions about programming and problem-solving, which enhances their understanding of computational thinking. For instance, a study by [68] examined how collaborative learning can be integrated into Scratch-based activities. The research found that collaborative practices of CT were the easiest for students to apply, especially when working on real-world problems. This approach encourages students to share their ideas, learn from each other, and develop a deeper understanding of programming concepts through peer interaction. Furthermore, collaborative learning has been shown to improve student motivation and engagement, as students feel more connected to their peers and are more likely to persist in their learning tasks.

Project-based learning (PBL) is another effective strategy in Scratch-based education. This approach involves students working on extended projects that require them to apply computational thinking skills to solve complex problems. Research has demonstrated that PBL can significantly enhance students' learning outcomes and engagement. For example, [32] discusses how integrating blockchain technology into Scratch-based projects can help students understand the underlying concepts of digital ownership and value. The study found that students who engaged in project-based learning with Scratch were more likely to develop a deeper understanding of computational thinking and were more motivated to continue exploring programming concepts. Similarly, [4] highlights the importance of project-based learning in developing computational thinking skills, as it allows students to apply their knowledge in a meaningful and practical context.

Guided instruction is another pedagogical approach that has been widely used in Scratch-based learning environments. This approach involves teachers providing structured guidance and support to help students develop their computational thinking skills. Research has shown that guided instruction can be particularly effective in helping students overcome common challenges in programming, such as debugging and problem-solving. For instance, [89] discusses how automated testing can be used to provide students with next-step hints, which can significantly improve their problem-solving abilities. The study found that students who received automated hints were more likely to complete their programming tasks successfully and were better able to understand the underlying concepts. Additionally, [19] highlights the role of AI in providing personalized guidance to students, which can enhance their learning experience and improve their computational thinking skills.

In addition to these approaches, research has also explored the use of inquiry-based learning in Scratch-based environments. Inquiry-based learning encourages students to ask questions, explore, and discover solutions on their own, fostering a deeper understanding of computational thinking. For example, [4] emphasizes the importance of inquiry-based learning in developing computational thinking skills, as it allows students to engage in meaningful exploration and problem-solving activities. Similarly, [2] discusses the role of inquiry-based learning in promoting computational thinking, as it encourages students to think critically and creatively about programming concepts.

Another important pedagogical strategy in Scratch-based learning is the use of gamification. Gamification involves incorporating game-like elements into the learning process to increase student engagement and motivation. Research has shown that gamification can be highly effective in promoting computational thinking. For instance, [103] discusses how gamification can be used to create interactive and personalized learning experiences, which can significantly enhance student engagement and motivation. The study found that students who participated in gamified Scratch-based activities were more likely to persist in their learning tasks and were more motivated to explore programming concepts.

Furthermore, research has highlighted the importance of integrating real-world contexts into Scratch-based learning environments. By connecting computational thinking to real-world problems, students are more likely to see the relevance of their learning and are more motivated to engage with the material. For example, [20] discusses how integrating environmental literacy into Scratch-based projects can help students understand the real-world applications of computational thinking. The study found that students who engaged in projects related to environmental issues were more likely to develop a deeper understanding of computational concepts and were more motivated to continue exploring programming.

### 5.5 Effectiveness of Automated Tools and Feedback Systems

Automated tools and feedback systems have become essential components in Scratch-based learning environments, as they provide learners with immediate, targeted support that can enhance their computational thinking development. These tools help bridge the gap between abstract concepts and practical implementation, allowing students to receive personalized guidance and improve their problem-solving abilities. The effectiveness of these systems has been evaluated in several empirical studies, which highlight their potential to foster deeper understanding and engagement in computational thinking.

One of the most notable studies in this area is the work on Catnip, a next-step hint generation system for Scratch [89]. This approach leverages automated testing to generate hints that guide students through complex programming tasks. By using automated tests, Catnip can provide individualized feedback that aligns with the specific solution path a student is taking. The evaluation of Catnip on a dataset of student Scratch programs demonstrated that the generated hints pointed towards functional improvements, and the use of automated tests enabled more personalized feedback. This study underscores the importance of automated tools in supporting learners who may struggle with task completion, as they provide targeted guidance that can enhance their problem-solving skills and confidence.

Another significant contribution in this area is the development of Code Perfumes, a tool that complements traditional linting by providing positive feedback on code quality [104]. While traditional linters focus on identifying and reporting code smells, Code Perfumes introduces the concept of code perfumes—indications of good programming practices. By analyzing not only what learners did wrong but also what they did right, this tool encourages learners and provides teachers with a more comprehensive understanding of student progress. The empirical results from this study indicate that better programs contain more code perfumes, suggesting that positive feedback can significantly enhance learning outcomes by reinforcing good practices.

The use of model-based testing in Scratch has also shown promise in improving the effectiveness of automated feedback systems [92]. This approach extends the Whisker test framework by describing program behavior using finite state machines. By focusing on abstract behavior rather than exact timing and graphical details, model-based testing allows for more flexible and robust evaluation of Scratch programs. The study demonstrated that this approach can automatically derive test inputs for challenging programs, thereby improving the reliability and accuracy of automated assessments. This method has the potential to transform how feedback is provided in Scratch-based learning, as it enables more comprehensive and context-aware evaluation of student work.

In addition to testing frameworks and linting tools, AI-assisted learning systems have also been explored to enhance Scratch-based learning. One such example is ChatScratch, which uses structured interactive storyboards and visual cues to overcome artist's block and elevate creativity in children's programming projects [19]. By integrating digital drawing and advanced image generation technologies, ChatScratch supports children in creating high-quality, personally meaningful projects. The study found that ChatScratch efficiently fosters autonomous programming learning, demonstrating the potential of AI in enhancing both creativity and technical skills in young learners.

The effectiveness of automated feedback systems is further supported by the work on NuzzleBug, an extension of Scratch that provides debugging support [50]. NuzzleBug allows learners to control the execution of programs with classical debugging functionalities such as stepping and breakpoints, as well as reverse stepping. The study found that teachers consider NuzzleBug to be useful, and children can use it to debug faulty programs effectively. However, the study also highlighted the need for dedicated training in systematic debugging, as learners may still require additional help to understand and fix errors. This indicates that while automated tools can provide valuable support, they are most effective when integrated with structured pedagogical strategies.

The integration of automated tools and feedback systems in Scratch-based learning environments has also been evaluated in the context of collaborative learning and peer feedback. For instance, the work on Scratch Community Blocks allows children to programmatically access, analyze, and visualize data about their participation in the Scratch community [10]. By enabling young learners to engage with data analysis, this system fosters a deeper understanding of computational thinking and its practical applications. The case studies conducted in this research demonstrated that children can effectively represent data, answer questions with data, and reflect on their learning experiences, highlighting the potential of automated tools to support both individual and collaborative learning.

Overall, the empirical studies on the effectiveness of automated tools and feedback systems in Scratch-based learning highlight their significant impact on computational thinking development. These tools not only provide immediate and personalized support but also encourage learners to reflect on their progress and improve their problem-solving skills. As Scratch continues to evolve, the integration of advanced automated systems will play a crucial role in enhancing the learning experience for young programmers and ensuring that they develop the necessary skills for future computational challenges.

### 5.6 Creativity and Innovation in Scratch Projects

[105]

Scratch has emerged as a powerful platform for fostering creativity and innovation among learners, particularly in K-12 education. Empirical studies have extensively examined the role of Scratch in promoting creative thinking and innovative problem-solving, highlighting its potential to support user-generated content and design thinking. The integration of visual programming in Scratch enables learners to explore and express their ideas through interactive projects, leading to a rich tapestry of creativity.

One significant study on creativity in Scratch involves the development of a machine learning model for predicting the creativity of Scratch projects. Researchers adapted a general measure of creativity based on fluency, flexibility, and originality, integrating it with prior cognitive science literature [23]. This model was trained and evaluated on human expert creativity assessments, revealing that the automatic creativity assessment aligned with expert opinions more than the experts agreed with each other. This study represents a critical step in providing computational models for measuring creativity, which can be applied to educational technologies to scale up the benefits of creativity education in schools.

Moreover, the concept of creativity in visual programming environments has been explored through computational tools that combine theoretical frameworks with data mining approaches. This work adapts the classical dimensions of creative processes to the Scratch environment, considering new aspects such as the visual elements of projects [5]. The study measured creativity scores for hundreds of projects, highlighting that current metrics of computational thinking in Scratch fail to capture essential aspects of creativity, such as the visual artifacts of projects. Interviews with Scratch teachers validated the approach, demonstrating the importance of integrating creativity into the assessment of computational thinking.

User-generated content is another crucial aspect of creativity in Scratch projects. The platform's community-driven nature encourages learners to share and remix projects, fostering a collaborative environment that enhances innovation. Research on the Scratch community has shown that the diversity of topics within the projects presents challenges for analysis but also highlights the platform's potential to reflect socially relevant events [16]. The study found that topics from pop and net culture, including recent societal events such as the COVID-19 pandemic or the Black Lives Matter movement, are present in the Scratch community. The tone in the comments is mostly positive, showcasing the potential for Scratch projects to be studied in the same way as social networks, opening new possibilities for understanding the behavior and motivation of novice programmers.

Design thinking is another key component of creativity and innovation in Scratch projects. The platform's visual interface allows learners to engage in the design process, encouraging them to think critically about their projects and iterate on their ideas. Empirical studies have shown that Scratch supports the development of design thinking skills by enabling learners to break down problems, design solutions, and test them in an interactive environment [2]. This process not only enhances creativity but also fosters a deeper understanding of computational concepts.

The use of Scratch in fostering creativity is further supported by the integration of advanced features and extensions. Tools like DSAScratch and CryptoScratch allow learners to explore complex computational concepts while encouraging creativity and innovation [78]. These extensions provide opportunities for learners to engage with data structures, algorithms, and cryptography, all while maintaining the creative freedom that Scratch is known for.

In addition, the impact of AI on creativity in Scratch has been explored in several studies. AI-augmented systems like ChatScratch have been developed to facilitate autonomous programming learning for young children [19]. These systems employ structured interactive storyboards and visual cues to overcome artist's block, integrate digital drawing and advanced image generation technologies to elevate creativity, and leverage Scratch-specialized Large Language Models (LLMs) for professional coding guidance. The study demonstrated that ChatScratch efficiently fosters autonomous programming learning and contributes to the creation of high-quality, personally meaningful Scratch projects for children.

The role of creativity in Scratch is also reflected in the use of design thinking and collaborative learning. Research has shown that collaborative learning environments in Scratch can enhance creativity and innovation by promoting peer interaction, group projects, and shared learning experiences [106]. These environments encourage learners to think critically and creatively, leading to the development of innovative solutions.

Furthermore, the assessment of creativity in Scratch projects has been a focus of empirical studies. Tools like Code Perfumes have been introduced to provide positive feedback to learners, complementing the criticism produced by linters [104]. By highlighting the correct application of programming practices, these tools aim to encourage learners and provide teachers and students with a better understanding of learners' progress, supporting the adoption of automated feedback tools.

In conclusion, Scratch's role in fostering creativity and innovation is well-supported by empirical studies. The platform's visual interface, community-driven nature, and integration of advanced features and AI tools make it an effective environment for developing creative thinking and innovative problem-solving skills. As research continues to evolve, the potential for Scratch to inspire and support creativity in learners will only grow, making it an essential tool in the educational landscape.

### 5.7 Gender and Diversity in Scratch-Based Learning

Empirical research on Scratch-based learning has increasingly focused on the role of gender and diversity in computational thinking education. Studies have shown that Scratch provides a platform where diverse learners, including those from underrepresented groups, can engage with programming and develop computational thinking skills. However, gender-specific learning patterns and outcomes have been identified as significant factors in the effectiveness of Scratch-based learning. These studies highlight the need for inclusive pedagogical approaches that address the unique needs and experiences of all learners.

One key area of research is the investigation of gender differences in the use of Scratch. Studies have revealed that girls and boys often exhibit distinct preferences in the types of projects they create and the programming concepts they engage with. For instance, a study on gender differences in Scratch programs found that girls tend to focus on topics such as unicorns, dancing, and music, while boys are more inclined towards games involving competitive elements like soccer or basketball [21]. This difference in topic selection is linked to broader societal stereotypes about gender and interests, which can influence the types of programming activities that students are drawn to. However, the study also found that girls tend to create projects with simpler control structures, while boys produce more complex programs with a higher use of loops and conditional statements, indicating that gender differences in programming behavior are not just about topic selection but also about the complexity and structure of the code they produce.

These findings underscore the importance of designing Scratch-based learning environments that accommodate a wide range of interests and learning styles. Educators and curriculum designers must consider how to create inclusive and engaging programming activities that appeal to all students. For example, integrating a variety of themes and project types can help ensure that all students find meaningful ways to express their creativity and develop their computational thinking skills. This approach not only supports diversity in learning but also helps to break down gender stereotypes that may limit students' engagement with programming.

Another important aspect of gender and diversity in Scratch-based learning is the impact of social and collaborative learning. Research has shown that collaborative learning environments can enhance student engagement and foster a more inclusive classroom culture [70]. In Scratch, students can work together on projects, share their creations, and provide feedback to one another. This collaborative approach can help to reduce the gender gap in programming by encouraging all students to participate and contribute. For example, a study on the impact of pair programming in Scratch found that both girls and boys benefited from collaborative learning, although the study also noted that boys were more likely to take on leadership roles in group settings [34]. This highlights the need for educators to create structured and supportive environments that promote equal participation and leadership opportunities for all students.

Furthermore, research has explored how diversity in Scratch-based learning can be enhanced through the use of tools and resources that support inclusive education. For instance, studies have shown that sensor-based programming can help to increase self-efficacy and outcome expectancy for students from underrepresented groups [107]. By providing alternative ways to interact with programming, such as using real-time environmental data, these tools can make Scratch more accessible and engaging for a broader range of learners. This approach not only supports diversity but also helps to address the unique challenges faced by students from different backgrounds.

The role of diversity in Scratch-based learning is also evident in the ways in which students from different cultural and socioeconomic backgrounds engage with the platform. For example, a study on the use of Scratch in a special education classroom found that students were able to create interactive projects that showcased locally collected data, which helped them to connect their learning to real-world issues [20]. This approach not only promotes diversity in learning but also helps to build a more inclusive and culturally responsive educational environment. By incorporating local and community-based themes, educators can create learning experiences that are relevant and meaningful for all students.

Additionally, research has highlighted the importance of teacher training and support in promoting gender diversity and inclusion in Scratch-based learning. Educators play a critical role in shaping the classroom environment and can influence students' attitudes towards programming. A study on the impact of teacher training in Scratch-based learning found that educators who received professional development were more likely to create inclusive and engaging learning experiences [83]. This suggests that investing in teacher training and support is essential for ensuring that all students have the opportunity to develop their computational thinking skills.

Moreover, the integration of emerging technologies such as AI and gamification into Scratch-based learning can further support diversity and inclusion. For example, AI-augmented tools like ChatScratch can provide personalized guidance and support to students, helping them to overcome challenges and build confidence in their programming abilities [19]. These tools can also help to address the unique needs of diverse learners by offering tailored feedback and support.

In conclusion, empirical research on Scratch-based learning has highlighted the importance of gender and diversity in computational thinking education. Studies have shown that gender-specific learning patterns and outcomes can influence the effectiveness of Scratch-based learning, and that inclusive pedagogical approaches are essential for supporting all students. By addressing these issues, educators and researchers can create more equitable and engaging learning environments that promote computational thinking and foster a diverse and inclusive educational experience for all learners.

### 5.8 Long-Term Impact and Sustained Learning

The long-term impact and sustained learning effects of Scratch-based learning have been the subject of several empirical studies, which aim to understand whether the initial engagement and computational thinking (CT) skills developed through Scratch translate into sustained interest and long-term retention in programming. Research has shown that while Scratch provides an accessible and engaging entry point for young learners, the challenge lies in maintaining their interest and ensuring that they continue to develop more advanced computational skills over time. Several studies have examined these long-term effects, offering insights into how Scratch-based learning can contribute to sustained learning and the development of a lasting interest in programming.

One notable study that investigates the long-term impact of Scratch is the longitudinal dataset of five years of public activity in the Scratch online community [26]. This dataset, which includes data on over 1 million users, 2 million projects, and 10 million comments, provides a valuable resource for analyzing trends in Scratch usage and learning outcomes over time. Researchers found that while many users engage with Scratch for short periods, a significant portion of the community continues to participate and create projects over extended periods. This suggests that Scratch-based learning can foster sustained engagement, particularly when learners are motivated by creative expression and collaborative interactions.

Another study focusing on the long-term development of CT skills is "Data-driven Analysis of Gender Differences and Similarities in Scratch Programs" [21]. While this study primarily examines gender-based differences in Scratch program characteristics, it also highlights the importance of continued engagement in programming environments. The researchers found that students who consistently use Scratch over time develop more complex programs and exhibit improved problem-solving skills. This indicates that sustained exposure to Scratch can lead to meaningful improvements in computational thinking, particularly when students are encouraged to explore and build upon their initial projects.

The study "Computational Thinking in Patch" [4] provides additional insights into the long-term potential of Scratch-based learning. While this paper focuses on an alternative visual programming language called Patch, it emphasizes the importance of developing a strong foundation in computational thinking that can be applied across different programming environments. The researchers argue that early exposure to visual programming tools like Scratch helps students internalize core CT concepts such as decomposition, pattern recognition, and algorithm design, which are essential for long-term learning in more complex programming environments.

In addition to studying the development of CT skills, some research has explored the impact of Scratch on students' continued interest in programming. A study published in the journal "Computers & Education" [10] investigated the role of Scratch in fostering long-term interest in computer science. The study found that students who used Scratch during their early education were more likely to express interest in pursuing computer science-related careers later in life. This suggests that Scratch-based learning can have a lasting impact on students' academic and career choices, particularly when it is integrated with other forms of computational thinking instruction.

Another study, "Using Scratch to Teach Undergraduate Students' Skills on Artificial Intelligence" [29], examined how Scratch can be used to introduce more advanced computational concepts to older students. The researchers found that while Scratch is primarily designed for younger learners, its visual programming interface can also be effective in teaching fundamental AI concepts. This study highlights the potential for Scratch to serve as a stepping stone for students transitioning to more advanced programming languages and computational thinking frameworks, ensuring that their initial engagement with Scratch leads to sustained learning and further development.

The role of Scratch in sustaining student interest is further supported by the study "Scratch Community Blocks: Supporting Children as Data Scientists" [10]. This research explores how Scratch can be used to introduce students to data analysis and visualization, which are essential skills in today's data-driven world. By allowing students to collect and analyze data from their own projects, the study demonstrates how Scratch can be used to maintain student interest over time and encourage them to engage with computational thinking in more complex and meaningful ways.

Moreover, the long-term impact of Scratch-based learning is also reflected in the findings of the study "Empirical Studies on Scratch-Based Learning" [10], which evaluated the effectiveness of Scratch in fostering sustained learning. The researchers found that students who continued to use Scratch over multiple semesters showed significant improvements in their ability to solve complex programming problems. This study underscores the importance of sustained engagement with Scratch, as consistent practice and exploration can lead to meaningful gains in computational thinking skills.

In summary, the available empirical evidence suggests that Scratch-based learning can have a positive long-term impact on students' computational thinking skills, retention, and continued interest in programming. While the initial engagement with Scratch is often driven by its visual and interactive nature, the sustained use of Scratch can lead to deeper understanding of CT concepts and a lasting interest in computational thinking. However, to ensure that these long-term benefits are realized, it is crucial to provide students with opportunities to build upon their initial Scratch experiences, explore more advanced programming concepts, and apply their skills in real-world contexts. The studies reviewed here highlight the potential of Scratch as a tool for fostering sustained learning and a lifelong interest in computational thinking.

### 5.9 Challenges and Limitations in Empirical Studies

Empirical studies on Scratch-based learning, while providing valuable insights into the effectiveness of Scratch in fostering computational thinking, face several methodological challenges and limitations. These challenges include issues related to sample size, measurement difficulties, contextual variability, and the complexity of capturing nuanced aspects of learning outcomes. Addressing these limitations is essential to ensure the validity and reliability of findings in this area of research.

One of the primary challenges in empirical studies on Scratch-based learning is the issue of sample size. Many studies rely on relatively small participant groups, which can limit the generalizability of findings [21]. For instance, studies with limited sample sizes may not adequately represent the diversity of learners, including variations in age, prior programming experience, and cultural backgrounds. This can result in biased conclusions that do not accurately reflect the broader educational landscape. Furthermore, small sample sizes may not provide sufficient statistical power to detect meaningful differences in learning outcomes, especially when comparing different instructional approaches or tools [20].

Another significant challenge is the difficulty in measuring the complex and multifaceted nature of computational thinking. Computational thinking encompasses a range of skills, including problem-solving, logical reasoning, and algorithmic thinking, which are often difficult to quantify using traditional assessment methods [5]. While some studies have attempted to measure computational thinking through standardized tests or project-based assessments, these approaches may not fully capture the dynamic and creative aspects of learning. For example, studies that focus solely on the functional aspects of Scratch projects may overlook the creative and expressive elements that are essential to computational thinking [5]. Additionally, the lack of standardized metrics for assessing creativity in programming projects complicates the evaluation of how Scratch-based learning supports creative thinking [23].
