As AI continues to evolve, it will play an increasingly important role in shaping how students learn, engage with, and apply CT concepts. From personalized learning experiences to collaborative learning environments, AI offers numerous opportunities to enhance CT education. By leveraging the power of AI, educators can create more inclusive, engaging, and effective learning environments that prepare students for the challenges of the digital age. However, it is crucial to approach the integration of AI in CT education with a thoughtful and ethical mindset, ensuring that these technologies are used to benefit all students and promote lifelong learning.

## 10 Future Directions and Innovations

### 10.1 Adaptive Learning Systems with AI Integration

Adaptive learning systems with AI integration represent a promising direction for the future of computational thinking education with Scratch. These systems leverage artificial intelligence to provide personalized learning experiences, adapting to individual student needs, preferences, and learning paces. By doing so, they aim to enhance student engagement, improve learning outcomes, and make computational thinking education more accessible and effective for diverse learners.

One of the key aspects of adaptive learning systems is their ability to dynamically adjust the difficulty and type of tasks presented to students based on their performance and progress. This personalization ensures that students are consistently challenged without becoming overwhelmed, which is crucial for maintaining engagement and fostering a growth mindset. For instance, systems can analyze student interactions with Scratch projects, identify areas of difficulty, and provide targeted support or additional practice opportunities. Research has shown that such personalized approaches can significantly improve student outcomes, as they allow learners to progress at their own pace and receive immediate, relevant feedback [101].

Moreover, AI integration enables adaptive learning systems to provide real-time feedback and guidance, which is particularly beneficial for students who may not have access to immediate human instruction. For example, AI-driven systems can offer hints, suggest alternative approaches, or explain concepts in multiple ways, catering to different learning styles. This level of support can be especially valuable in informal learning environments, such as after-school programs or self-directed learning, where students may not have consistent access to teachers. The use of AI in feedback systems has been explored in several studies, highlighting the potential for these technologies to enhance the learning experience and improve computational thinking skills [101].

Another important feature of adaptive learning systems is their ability to analyze and interpret large amounts of data to identify patterns and trends in student performance. By leveraging machine learning algorithms, these systems can detect common misconceptions, track progress over time, and predict future learning needs. This data-driven approach allows educators to make informed decisions about curriculum design, instructional strategies, and student support. For instance, educators can use insights from adaptive systems to identify students who may need additional support or to adjust teaching methods to better align with student needs [101].

The integration of AI into adaptive learning systems also opens up new possibilities for collaborative learning and social interaction. AI can facilitate peer-to-peer learning by connecting students with similar interests or complementary strengths, enabling them to work together on projects and share knowledge. Additionally, AI can monitor group activities, provide real-time feedback, and suggest ways to improve collaboration and communication. This can be particularly beneficial in collaborative learning environments, where the goal is to develop not only computational thinking skills but also teamwork and communication abilities [68].

Furthermore, adaptive learning systems can support the development of creativity and innovation by allowing students to explore different solutions and experiment with various approaches. AI can provide creative suggestions, generate examples, or offer alternative perspectives, encouraging students to think outside the box and develop their own unique solutions. This aspect is particularly relevant in the context of Scratch, which is designed to promote creativity and self-expression through programming [61].

The potential of adaptive learning systems with AI integration is not limited to individualized learning experiences. They can also be used to support teacher training and professional development, providing educators with insights into student learning and helping them refine their teaching strategies. For example, AI-driven analytics can help teachers identify areas where students are struggling, allowing them to adjust their instruction and provide more targeted support. Additionally, adaptive systems can offer training modules, resources, and best practices to help teachers effectively integrate computational thinking into their classrooms [68].

Despite the many benefits of adaptive learning systems with AI integration, there are also challenges and considerations that must be addressed. One of the main challenges is ensuring that these systems are accessible to all students, regardless of their socioeconomic background or technological resources. Additionally, there is a need to develop ethical guidelines and standards for the use of AI in education, ensuring that these systems are transparent, fair, and unbiased. Furthermore, there is a need for ongoing research and evaluation to assess the effectiveness of adaptive learning systems and to identify best practices for their implementation [136].

In conclusion, adaptive learning systems with AI integration hold great promise for the future of computational thinking education with Scratch. By personalizing the learning experience, providing real-time feedback, and supporting collaborative and creative learning, these systems can enhance student engagement, improve learning outcomes, and make computational thinking more accessible to a wider audience. However, to fully realize the potential of these systems, it is essential to address the challenges related to accessibility, ethics, and evaluation, and to continue exploring new ways to integrate AI into computational thinking education. As research in this area continues to evolve, the role of adaptive learning systems with AI integration is likely to become even more significant in shaping the future of computational thinking education [101].

### 10.2 Interdisciplinary Computational Thinking Approaches

The integration of computational thinking (CT) across different disciplines such as science, mathematics, and the arts is a promising avenue for fostering a more holistic and meaningful educational experience. This interdisciplinary approach not only enhances students' understanding of CT but also allows them to apply these skills in real-world contexts, promoting deeper learning and creativity. By leveraging tools like Scratch, educators can create learning environments that bridge multiple domains, making CT more accessible and relevant to a diverse range of learners.

One of the key ways in which CT can be integrated with science is through the use of simulation and modeling. For instance, in science education, students can use Scratch to create simulations of natural phenomena, such as weather patterns, ecological systems, or physical processes. These simulations allow students to explore complex scientific concepts in an interactive and engaging way, fostering both computational thinking and scientific understanding. Research has shown that when students are given the opportunity to model real-world phenomena, they are more likely to develop a deeper understanding of the underlying scientific principles [88]. This approach not only reinforces CT skills but also encourages students to think critically about the relationship between computation and the natural world.

In the realm of mathematics, CT can be used to enhance students' problem-solving abilities and mathematical reasoning. Scratch provides a platform for students to explore mathematical concepts such as geometry, algebra, and data analysis through visual programming. For example, students can create interactive games or animations that require them to apply mathematical concepts such as coordinates, angles, and functions. This hands-on approach to mathematics helps students see the relevance of CT in solving real-world problems and encourages them to think abstractly and algorithmically. Studies have shown that integrating CT into mathematics education can lead to significant improvements in students' problem-solving skills and mathematical fluency [4].

The arts also offer a rich context for integrating CT, as both fields emphasize creativity, design, and expression. In the arts, students can use Scratch to create digital art, music, and interactive stories, combining computational thinking with artistic creativity. This interdisciplinary approach not only fosters technical skills but also encourages students to think about the aesthetic and emotional aspects of their work. Research has highlighted the potential of visual programming environments like Scratch to promote creative thinking and self-expression, as they allow students to experiment with different forms of media and develop their own unique projects [5]. By merging CT with the arts, educators can create learning experiences that are both intellectually stimulating and personally meaningful.

Furthermore, interdisciplinary approaches to CT can help address the diverse needs and interests of students. For example, in a classroom that includes students with varying levels of mathematical and scientific knowledge, integrating CT with other subjects can provide multiple entry points for learning. This approach allows students to engage with CT concepts in ways that are relevant to their own interests and experiences, promoting inclusivity and equity in education. Research has shown that when students are given the opportunity to connect CT with their personal interests, they are more likely to stay engaged and motivated [47].

Another benefit of interdisciplinary CT is the potential to foster collaboration and teamwork. In collaborative learning environments, students from different disciplines can work together on projects that require a combination of CT, scientific inquiry, mathematical reasoning, and artistic expression. This type of collaborative learning not only enhances students' CT skills but also helps them develop communication, problem-solving, and project management skills. Studies have shown that when students engage in collaborative learning, they are more likely to develop a deeper understanding of the material and to retain what they have learned [2].

Moreover, interdisciplinary CT can help bridge the gap between different educational domains, making learning more cohesive and meaningful. For example, in a science class, students might use CT to analyze data and create visualizations, while in an art class, they might use CT to create interactive installations. This integration of CT across different subjects helps students see the connections between different areas of knowledge and understand how CT can be applied in a variety of contexts. Research has shown that when students are exposed to CT in multiple contexts, they are better able to transfer their skills to new situations and apply them in meaningful ways [4].

In addition to enhancing students' understanding of CT, interdisciplinary approaches can also help address the challenges of teaching CT in diverse educational settings. For example, in classrooms where students have limited access to technology or where teachers may not have extensive training in CT, integrating CT with other subjects can provide a more flexible and accessible approach to teaching. This approach allows teachers to use familiar tools and methods from other disciplines to introduce CT concepts in a way that is engaging and relevant to students [137].

Finally, interdisciplinary CT has the potential to inspire new forms of educational innovation and research. As educators and researchers continue to explore the intersections between CT and other disciplines, they may develop new teaching strategies, tools, and curricula that can be used to enhance learning across a wide range of subjects. This ongoing exploration can lead to the creation of more effective and inclusive educational experiences that prepare students for the challenges of the 21st century [2]. By embracing an interdisciplinary approach to CT, educators can help students develop the skills and knowledge they need to thrive in an increasingly complex and interconnected world.

### 10.3 AI-Augmented Creative Learning Environments

AI-augmented creative learning environments represent a significant frontier in the evolution of computational thinking education, particularly through the integration of tools like ChatScratch. These environments leverage the power of artificial intelligence to enhance creativity, autonomy, and the overall learning experience for children engaging with Scratch-based programming. The potential of AI-augmented tools to support children in developing more complex and meaningful projects is increasingly evident as these technologies mature and become more accessible. By incorporating AI, these platforms not only provide immediate feedback and guidance but also foster a deeper understanding of programming concepts, encouraging learners to explore and experiment in ways that were previously difficult or impossible with traditional block-based programming tools [19].

One of the key areas where AI augmentation can make a substantial impact is in addressing the challenges of artist's block and bounded creativity, which are common among young learners. ChatScratch, for instance, addresses these issues by employing structured interactive storyboards and visual cues to guide children in planning their projects. The system also integrates digital drawing and advanced image generation technologies to elevate creativity, allowing children to visualize and implement their ideas more effectively [19]. This not only helps in overcoming initial creative hurdles but also encourages children to think critically about their project designs and explore a wider range of creative possibilities. By providing a supportive environment that fosters creativity, AI-augmented tools like ChatScratch can significantly enhance the quality and originality of projects developed by children.

Another critical aspect of AI-augmented creative learning environments is the provision of professional coding guidance. Traditional block-based environments, while user-friendly, often lack the depth and nuance needed to support complex programming tasks. AI-augmented systems such as ChatScratch leverage Scratch-specialized Large Language Models (LLMs) to offer intelligent and context-aware assistance, helping children navigate the complexities of programming. This support is particularly crucial in ensuring that children can translate their creative ideas into functional and well-structured programs. By providing real-time feedback and suggestions, AI-augmented tools help learners refine their code, correct errors, and develop a more profound understanding of programming concepts [19].

Moreover, AI-augmented tools can play a vital role in fostering autonomy among young learners. By offering personalized guidance and support, these systems enable children to take greater control over their learning process. This is particularly beneficial in environments where children may not have access to expert mentors or traditional classroom settings. AI-augmented systems can adapt to the individual needs and learning styles of each child, providing tailored feedback and resources that enhance the learning experience. For example, tools like ChatScratch not only assist with project planning and coding but also encourage children to reflect on their learning journey and set personal goals. This level of personalization can significantly increase motivation and engagement, leading to more meaningful and impactful learning outcomes [19].

In addition to supporting individual learning, AI-augmented creative learning environments can also facilitate collaborative learning and social interaction. By integrating AI-driven feedback mechanisms, these platforms can help learners work together more effectively, sharing ideas and building on each other's strengths. For instance, AI can assist in identifying areas where collaboration is most beneficial and provide suggestions for team-based activities that promote both creativity and problem-solving. This not only enhances the learning experience but also helps children develop essential social and teamwork skills. Furthermore, AI can analyze and provide insights into group dynamics, helping educators and facilitators create more inclusive and effective collaborative learning environments [10].

The integration of AI into creative learning environments also opens up new possibilities for assessing and enhancing creativity. Traditional methods of assessing creativity in programming projects often rely on subjective judgments and manual evaluations, which can be time-consuming and inconsistent. AI-augmented tools can provide objective and data-driven assessments of creativity, using machine learning models to analyze various aspects of a project, such as originality, complexity, and the use of visual elements. For example, the work of [5] demonstrates how computational tools can measure creativity in visual programming environments, providing valuable insights into the creative processes of young learners. By leveraging AI, these assessments can be more accurate and scalable, allowing educators to better understand and support the creative development of their students.

In conclusion, AI-augmented creative learning environments hold immense potential to transform the way children engage with computational thinking and programming. Tools like ChatScratch exemplify how AI can be harnessed to enhance creativity, provide personalized guidance, and foster autonomy among young learners. By addressing key challenges such as artist's block and bounded creativity, these environments not only support the development of complex and meaningful projects but also promote a deeper understanding of programming concepts. As these technologies continue to evolve, they will play an increasingly important role in shaping the future of computational thinking education, making it more accessible, engaging, and effective for all learners. [19]

### 10.4 Collaborative and Social Learning in Computational Thinking

Collaborative and social learning models have long been recognized as essential components of effective educational practices, particularly in the context of computational thinking (CT). As the educational landscape evolves, emerging technologies are increasingly being leveraged to enhance these models, making them more dynamic, inclusive, and impactful. In the context of Scratch, a block-based programming environment that has gained widespread popularity in K-12 education, the integration of collaborative and social learning strategies is not only beneficial but also transformative. Through peer-to-peer learning and group projects, students are not only developing their CT skills but also fostering a sense of community and shared purpose. Emerging technologies such as AI, gamification, and social media platforms are further enhancing these collaborative learning models, offering new opportunities for students to engage with CT in meaningful ways.

One of the most significant ways in which collaborative learning is being enhanced in Scratch is through the use of peer-to-peer interactions. Research has shown that when students work together, they are more likely to engage in deeper cognitive processes, such as problem-solving and critical thinking. In a study focusing on collaborative programming with the micro:bit device, it was found that secondary school girls perceived collaborative practices of CT as the easiest to apply, indicating the potential of peer-to-peer learning to foster CT skills [67]. This suggests that collaborative learning environments can significantly enhance students' ability to develop and apply CT skills, especially when they are working in groups and sharing their ideas and solutions.

Group projects in Scratch also provide a rich context for collaborative learning, as students are encouraged to work together on complex programming tasks. These projects often require students to divide tasks, communicate effectively, and integrate their individual contributions into a cohesive whole. The social interaction that occurs during these projects not only helps students develop CT skills but also enhances their communication and teamwork abilities. For example, the Scratch Community Blocks system allows children to programmatically access, analyze, and visualize data about their participation in the Scratch community, fostering a sense of shared ownership and collaboration among learners [10]. This kind of data-driven collaboration not only supports CT development but also encourages students to think critically about their own learning processes and the learning of others.

Emerging technologies are further enhancing these collaborative and social learning models by providing new tools and platforms for interaction. AI, for instance, can be used to create intelligent tutoring systems that provide personalized feedback and guidance to students as they work on group projects. These systems can monitor students' progress, identify areas where they may be struggling, and offer targeted support, all while fostering a collaborative environment. The use of AI in educational settings is not only improving the efficiency of learning but also making it more accessible and inclusive. For example, the integration of ChatScratch, an AI-augmented system that facilitates autonomous programming learning for young children, has been shown to enhance students' ability to create high-quality, personally meaningful Scratch projects [19]. This type of AI-driven support can be particularly valuable in collaborative settings, where students may need additional guidance and encouragement.

Gamification is another emerging technology that is being used to enhance collaborative and social learning in CT. By incorporating game-like elements into learning activities, educators can increase student engagement and motivation, making the learning process more enjoyable and effective. In the context of Scratch, gamification can be used to create interactive challenges and competitions that encourage students to work together and share their knowledge. For example, the use of AI-assisted hint generation tools can provide students with real-time feedback and support as they work on programming tasks, making the learning experience more dynamic and engaging [89]. These tools not only help students overcome challenges but also encourage them to think creatively and collaboratively.

Social media platforms are also playing an increasingly important role in collaborative and social learning in CT. These platforms provide students with opportunities to share their projects, receive feedback from peers, and engage in discussions about computational thinking. The Scratch online community, for instance, allows students to share their projects with a global audience, fostering a sense of community and shared purpose. This kind of social interaction not only enhances students' CT skills but also encourages them to think critically about their work and the work of others. Research has shown that students who actively participate in online communities are more likely to develop a deeper understanding of CT concepts and to apply them in meaningful ways [16].

In addition to peer-to-peer learning and group projects, emerging technologies are also enabling new forms of collaborative learning that go beyond traditional classroom settings. For example, the use of AI in educational settings is allowing for the creation of virtual learning environments where students can interact with intelligent agents and other students in real-time. These environments can be particularly effective in fostering collaborative learning, as they provide students with opportunities to work together on complex tasks and share their knowledge. The integration of AI into these environments is not only enhancing the learning experience but also making it more personalized and adaptive to the needs of individual students.

In conclusion, the integration of collaborative and social learning models with emerging technologies is significantly enhancing the development of computational thinking skills in Scratch. Peer-to-peer learning, group projects, and the use of AI, gamification, and social media platforms are all contributing to a more dynamic and inclusive learning environment. By leveraging these technologies, educators can create rich, interactive experiences that foster CT skills, promote collaboration, and encourage students to think critically and creatively. As the field of computational thinking education continues to evolve, the role of collaborative and social learning will only become more important, offering new opportunities for students to engage with CT in meaningful and impactful ways.

### 10.5 Ethical and Inclusive AI in Computational Thinking Education

The integration of Artificial Intelligence (AI) into computational thinking education, particularly within Scratch-based learning environments, brings with it a complex set of ethical, accessibility, and inclusivity challenges that must be addressed to ensure equitable benefits for all students. As AI becomes increasingly embedded in educational technologies, it is critical to examine its implications for fairness, transparency, and the diverse needs of learners. This subsection explores these ethical considerations, emphasizing the need to foster inclusivity and ensure that AI-driven tools in computational thinking education do not perpetuate biases or exclude underrepresented groups.

One of the primary ethical concerns in AI-driven computational thinking education is the issue of fairness. AI systems, particularly those involving large language models (LLMs) and automated feedback mechanisms, can inadvertently perpetuate biases present in their training data. For example, research on AI-assisted coding tools such as ChatScratch has highlighted the importance of ensuring that AI-generated suggestions and feedback are not skewed toward certain types of problem-solving strategies or user demographics [19]. This raises the question of whether AI-driven tools may favor certain learning styles, cultural backgrounds, or gender identities, thereby reinforcing existing inequalities. To address this, developers of AI tools for Scratch-based learning must prioritize fairness-aware algorithms that are trained on diverse datasets and regularly audited for bias.

Accessibility is another crucial ethical consideration in the integration of AI into computational thinking education. While AI-powered tools can enhance learning experiences, they may also create new barriers for students with disabilities or those who lack access to advanced technological resources. For instance, tools like LitterBox, which automates the detection of bugs in Scratch programs, may not be fully accessible to students with visual impairments or those who rely on screen readers [9]. Similarly, AI-driven tutoring systems that rely on natural language processing may be less effective for students with limited English proficiency or those who require alternative communication methods. Therefore, it is essential to ensure that AI tools are designed with universal design principles in mind, incorporating features such as adjustable interfaces, multilingual support, and compatibility with assistive technologies.

Inclusivity is a key component of ethical AI in computational thinking education, as it ensures that all students, regardless of their background, have equal opportunities to engage with and benefit from AI-enhanced learning environments. Scratch has long been recognized for its ability to democratize access to programming education, enabling students from diverse socioeconomic backgrounds to explore computational thinking in a supportive and creative environment [10]. However, the introduction of AI into Scratch-based learning must be carefully managed to avoid creating new forms of exclusion. For example, AI-powered tools that generate personalized learning paths or adaptive feedback may inadvertently disadvantage students who lack access to high-speed internet or modern devices [16]. To mitigate these risks, educators and developers must work together to ensure that AI-driven tools are accessible, affordable, and usable across different learning contexts.

Moreover, the ethical integration of AI in Scratch-based learning requires a commitment to transparency and user autonomy. Students and educators should have clear information about how AI systems operate, what data they collect, and how this data is used to support learning. Tools like Code Perfumes, which provide positive feedback to learners by highlighting good coding practices, demonstrate the potential of AI to support students in a constructive and non-judgmental way [104]. However, the use of AI in educational settings must also be accompanied by mechanisms for user control, such as the ability to opt out of certain features or to customize AI-generated recommendations.

Another important ethical dimension is the need to protect student privacy in AI-enhanced computational thinking environments. As AI systems collect and analyze data to provide personalized learning experiences, there is a risk that sensitive information may be mishandled or misused. For example, tools like Scratch Community Blocks, which allow children to analyze and visualize data about their participation in the Scratch community, raise questions about data ownership and consent [10]. To address these concerns, developers must implement robust data protection measures, such as encryption, anonymization, and clear privacy policies, while also involving students and parents in decisions about data usage.

In addition to these ethical considerations, the inclusion of AI in Scratch-based learning must also take into account the cultural and social contexts of learners. AI-driven tools that are designed without consideration for local cultures, languages, or educational practices may fail to resonate with students and may even be perceived as alienating or irrelevant. For example, studies on gender-specific programming behaviors in Scratch have shown that girls and boys may approach programming tasks differently, with girls often preferring projects centered around creativity and storytelling [21]. AI systems that fail to account for these differences may inadvertently reinforce stereotypes or discourage certain groups from engaging with computational thinking. Therefore, it is essential to design AI tools that are culturally responsive and adaptable to the diverse needs of learners.

Finally, the ethical integration of AI in computational thinking education requires a commitment to ongoing reflection and improvement. As AI technologies continue to evolve, it is crucial to regularly evaluate their impact on students, educators, and educational systems. This includes monitoring for unintended consequences, such as increased disparities in learning outcomes or the over-reliance on AI-driven feedback at the expense of human guidance. By fostering a culture of ethical awareness and continuous improvement, the educational community can ensure that AI serves as a powerful tool for enhancing computational thinking, rather than a source of inequity or exclusion.

In conclusion, the ethical and inclusive integration of AI into Scratch-based computational thinking education is essential to ensure that all students benefit from these technological advancements. By addressing issues of fairness, accessibility, inclusivity, transparency, and privacy, educators and developers can create AI-driven learning environments that are equitable, empowering, and aligned with the diverse needs of learners. This requires a collaborative effort to design, implement, and refine AI tools in a way that promotes both innovation and social responsibility.

### 10.6 Computational Thinking and AI Literacy

Computational thinking (CT) and artificial intelligence (AI) literacy are two critical domains in modern education, especially as technology continues to reshape the way we live and work. The intersection of these two fields presents a unique opportunity to enhance students’ understanding of how computational processes underpin AI systems and how AI can be leveraged to support and augment computational thinking. Scratch, a block-based programming environment, has emerged as a powerful tool for introducing students to programming concepts, and it can also serve as a foundation for teaching AI literacy. By integrating AI concepts into Scratch-based learning, educators can help students grasp not only the mechanics of programming but also the broader implications of AI in society. This subsection explores how Scratch can be used to teach AI literacy, emphasizing its role in fostering a deeper understanding of AI principles and their real-world applications.

One of the key aspects of AI literacy is understanding how AI systems operate and how they make decisions. Scratch provides a visual and accessible platform for students to experiment with AI concepts, even at a basic level. For instance, tools like ChatScratch [19] integrate AI technologies such as large language models (LLMs) to support learners in creating and debugging programs. These systems can guide students through the creative process, offering hints, suggesting improvements, and even generating code based on high-level descriptions. By engaging with such tools, students begin to understand how AI can assist in problem-solving and how it interacts with computational processes. This exposure is crucial in developing AI literacy, as it helps students recognize the potential and limitations of AI in programming and beyond.

Moreover, Scratch can be used to introduce students to the ethical and societal implications of AI. As AI systems become more pervasive, it is essential for students to understand the ethical considerations involved in their development and deployment. Scratch-based activities can be designed to explore topics such as bias in algorithms, the importance of data privacy, and the impact of AI on employment and social equity. For example, the study by [2] emphasizes the importance of integrating interdisciplinary approaches into computational thinking education. By combining CT with ethical and societal perspectives, students can develop a more nuanced understanding of AI and its role in the world.

Another important aspect of AI literacy is the ability to critically evaluate AI systems and their outputs. Scratch can support this by enabling students to create simple AI models and analyze their behavior. For instance, students can use Scratch to build basic machine learning models, such as image classifiers or recommendation systems, and observe how these models perform under different conditions. This hands-on experience allows students to understand how AI systems learn from data and how their decisions can be influenced by the quality and diversity of the data they are trained on. Such activities align with the principles of computational thinking, as they require students to break down complex problems, design solutions, and test their effectiveness in a systematic way.

Additionally, the integration of AI into Scratch-based learning can help students develop a deeper understanding of the relationship between programming and AI. While traditional programming focuses on writing explicit instructions for a computer to follow, AI involves training systems to make decisions based on data. Scratch provides a platform for students to explore this distinction by experimenting with AI-driven programs, such as those that use machine learning algorithms to adapt to user input. For example, the work by [29] demonstrates how Scratch can be used to introduce students to fundamental AI concepts such as clustering and neural networks. By building and testing these models, students gain insights into how AI systems operate and how they can be applied to solve real-world problems.

Furthermore, Scratch can serve as a bridge between computational thinking and AI literacy by fostering creativity and innovation. The ability to think creatively is a key component of both CT and AI, as it enables individuals to develop novel solutions to complex problems. Scratch's visual and interactive nature encourages students to experiment with different approaches, leading to the creation of unique and meaningful projects. This creative process can be extended to AI by challenging students to design AI-powered applications that address real-world challenges. For instance, students might use Scratch to create AI-driven games that adapt to player behavior or develop AI-based tools for analyzing and visualizing data. Such projects not only reinforce computational thinking skills but also provide students with a deeper understanding of the practical applications of AI.

In addition to these benefits, the use of Scratch to teach AI literacy can also help address the growing demand for AI education in K-12 settings. As AI becomes increasingly integrated into various aspects of daily life, it is essential for students to develop a foundational understanding of AI concepts and their implications. Scratch provides an accessible and engaging way for students to explore these ideas, making it an ideal tool for introducing AI literacy in the classroom. By incorporating AI into Scratch-based learning, educators can help students build the skills and knowledge they need to navigate an increasingly AI-driven world.

In conclusion, the intersection of computational thinking and AI literacy presents a valuable opportunity to enhance students’ understanding of both domains. Scratch, with its visual and interactive design, offers a powerful platform for introducing students to AI concepts and their real-world applications. By integrating AI into Scratch-based learning, educators can help students develop a deeper understanding of how AI systems operate, the ethical considerations involved, and the creative possibilities that arise from their use. As the field of AI continues to evolve, it is essential that education keeps pace by equipping students with the knowledge and skills they need to engage with and shape the future of AI. Through the use of Scratch, this goal becomes not only achievable but also an exciting and engaging learning experience for students of all ages.

### 10.7 Future Pedagogical Models and Teacher Training

As computational thinking (CT) becomes an essential component of modern education, the integration of AI and CT into Scratch-based learning environments presents both challenges and opportunities. Future pedagogical models and teacher training strategies must evolve to prepare educators to effectively utilize these tools. This subsection explores emerging pedagogical models and training strategies that aim to enhance the integration of AI and CT in Scratch-based learning, drawing upon insights from current research and addressing the need for scalable, inclusive, and effective approaches.

One promising direction for future pedagogical models is the development of AI-augmented learning environments that support both computational thinking and creative expression. The integration of AI into Scratch-based learning can provide intelligent tutoring systems that offer personalized guidance, adaptive feedback, and real-time assistance to students [19]. For instance, systems like ChatScratch leverage Large Language Models (LLMs) to offer creative and technical support, enabling young learners to develop more complex and meaningful projects. Such AI-augmented tools can not only assist students in overcoming technical barriers but also foster a more engaging and self-directed learning experience. However, the successful implementation of these tools requires educators to understand how to integrate AI into their teaching practices, which necessitates a shift in pedagogical approaches.

Another critical area for future pedagogical models is the development of interdisciplinary curricula that integrate computational thinking with other subjects. Research has shown that interdisciplinary approaches can enhance student engagement and deepen their understanding of complex concepts [88]. For example, integrating CT with mathematics, science, or the arts can provide students with a more holistic learning experience. In such models, Scratch can serve as a platform to explore and apply computational thinking in real-world contexts, promoting both technical and creative skills. However, these interdisciplinary approaches require educators to develop new teaching strategies and curricula that align with both CT and the subject-specific learning goals.

Teacher training is a crucial component of preparing educators to effectively integrate AI and computational thinking into Scratch-based learning. Current studies highlight the importance of professional development programs that equip teachers with the necessary knowledge and skills to use Scratch and AI tools in the classroom [50; 24]. For instance, tools like LitterBox and NuzzleBug provide automated testing and debugging support, which can help teachers monitor student progress and provide targeted feedback. However, these tools are only effective if teachers understand how to use them and integrate them into their teaching practices. Therefore, future teacher training programs should focus on building teachers’ competence in using these tools, as well as their ability to design and implement CT-based lessons.

In addition, future pedagogical models should emphasize collaborative and social learning environments. Research has demonstrated that collaborative learning, such as pair programming and group projects, can enhance students’ problem-solving skills and deepen their understanding of computational thinking [34; 70]. In Scratch-based learning, collaborative projects can encourage students to share ideas, work together on coding tasks, and engage in peer review. These collaborative models can be further enhanced by integrating AI tools that support group dynamics and provide real-time feedback on collaborative activities. For example, AI-driven systems could analyze group interactions and suggest strategies to improve collaboration or provide insights into individual contributions.

Furthermore, future pedagogical models should address the need for inclusive and accessible learning experiences. Research has shown that students from underrepresented groups, including girls and students from diverse socioeconomic backgrounds, may face unique challenges in developing computational thinking skills [107; 138]. Therefore, future teacher training strategies should focus on equipping educators with the knowledge and strategies to create inclusive learning environments that support all students. This includes understanding gender-specific learning behaviors, addressing accessibility issues, and promoting equitable opportunities for all learners.

Another important aspect of future pedagogical models is the integration of data-driven approaches to assess and improve learning outcomes. Tools like EdNet, a large-scale hierarchical dataset of student interactions, provide valuable insights into how students learn and engage with computational thinking [112]. By analyzing data from Scratch-based learning environments, educators can gain a deeper understanding of student progress, identify learning gaps, and tailor their instruction accordingly. Future teacher training should include training on data analysis and interpretation, enabling educators to leverage these insights to improve their teaching practices.

Moreover, future pedagogical models should explore the use of adaptive learning systems that personalize the learning experience for each student. AI-driven systems can analyze student performance and adjust the difficulty of tasks, provide targeted feedback, and suggest learning pathways that align with individual student needs [113]. Such adaptive systems can help educators manage diverse classrooms more effectively and ensure that all students receive the support they need to succeed. However, the implementation of these systems requires educators to understand how to integrate them into their teaching and how to interpret the data they provide.

Finally, future pedagogical models should emphasize the importance of continuous professional development for educators. The rapid evolution of AI and computational thinking tools requires teachers to stay updated on the latest developments and best practices. Professional development programs should include opportunities for educators to collaborate, share experiences, and learn from each other. This can be facilitated through online communities, workshops, and other professional learning networks that support ongoing growth and innovation in teaching practices.

In conclusion, the future of pedagogical models and teacher training in Scratch-based learning environments must focus on the integration of AI and computational thinking. By developing interdisciplinary curricula, enhancing collaborative learning, promoting inclusivity, leveraging data-driven approaches, and supporting continuous professional development, educators can better prepare students for the challenges and opportunities of a technology-driven world. The integration of AI tools like ChatScratch and the use of data analytics platforms like EdNet can further enhance these efforts, providing educators with the resources they need to create engaging and effective learning experiences. As these models and strategies continue to evolve, they will play a critical role in shaping the future of computational thinking education.

### 10.8 Scalable and Sustainable Educational Platforms

The development of scalable and sustainable educational platforms is crucial for the long-term success of Scratch-based computational thinking (CT) education. As Scratch continues to gain traction in K-12 and higher education settings, the need for platforms that can support large-scale adoption while maintaining quality and accessibility becomes increasingly apparent. Emerging technologies offer promising avenues to achieve this goal, enabling the creation of platforms that are not only scalable but also sustainable in the face of evolving educational needs and technological advancements. 

One of the key challenges in scaling Scratch-based CT education is the need for robust infrastructure that can handle large numbers of users, while also providing personalized learning experiences. This is where emerging technologies such as cloud computing and artificial intelligence (AI) come into play. Cloud-based platforms can offer the necessary scalability to support a growing user base, ensuring that all learners have access to the resources they need. AI, on the other hand, can help personalize the learning experience by adapting to individual student needs and providing targeted feedback. For instance, AI-driven tutoring systems can analyze student performance and adjust the difficulty of tasks in real-time, ensuring that learners are appropriately challenged without becoming overwhelmed [90].

Another important aspect of scalable and sustainable platforms is the integration of open-source technologies and community-driven development. Open-source platforms allow for greater flexibility and customization, enabling educators and developers to tailor the learning environment to meet the specific needs of their students. Additionally, community-driven development fosters a sense of ownership and collaboration, encouraging continuous improvement and innovation. For example, the Scratch community itself is a prime example of how a collaborative, open-source approach can lead to the creation of a vibrant and sustainable learning environment [10].

In addition to scalability and sustainability, accessibility is a critical factor in the development of educational platforms. Emerging technologies such as machine learning and natural language processing (NLP) can be leveraged to create more inclusive learning environments. For instance, NLP can be used to develop tools that assist students with disabilities, providing them with alternative ways to interact with the learning material. This not only enhances the learning experience for all students but also ensures that the platform is accessible to a wider audience. Furthermore, the use of adaptive learning technologies can help bridge the gap between students with varying levels of prior knowledge and skills, ensuring that everyone has the opportunity to succeed [90].

The integration of emerging technologies such as blockchain and distributed ledger technologies can also contribute to the scalability and sustainability of educational platforms. Blockchain can be used to create secure, transparent, and tamper-proof records of student learning achievements, ensuring that credentials and milestones are accurately documented and easily accessible. This not only enhances the integrity of the learning process but also facilitates the sharing of credentials across different educational institutions and platforms. For example, blockchain-based systems can be used to track student progress in computational thinking, providing a comprehensive and reliable record of their learning journey [32].

Moreover, the use of data analytics and learning analytics can play a significant role in the development of scalable and sustainable educational platforms. By collecting and analyzing data on student interactions, educators can gain valuable insights into how learners engage with the material and identify areas where additional support may be needed. This data-driven approach can help optimize the learning experience, ensuring that resources are allocated effectively and that interventions are targeted where they are most needed. For instance, learning analytics can be used to monitor student engagement and motivation, enabling educators to adjust their teaching strategies to better meet the needs of their students [116].

Another important consideration in the development of scalable and sustainable platforms is the need for continuous evaluation and improvement. This involves not only assessing the effectiveness of the platform in achieving its educational goals but also ensuring that it remains up-to-date with the latest advancements in technology and pedagogy. For example, platforms can be designed to incorporate user feedback and iterative improvements, ensuring that they remain relevant and effective over time. This approach not only enhances the quality of the learning experience but also fosters a culture of continuous innovation and improvement [91].

In conclusion, the development of scalable and sustainable educational platforms is essential for the continued growth and success of Scratch-based computational thinking education. By leveraging emerging technologies such as cloud computing, AI, and blockchain, educational platforms can be designed to support large-scale adoption while maintaining quality and accessibility. Additionally, the integration of open-source technologies, community-driven development, and data analytics can further enhance the scalability and sustainability of these platforms. As the field of computational thinking education continues to evolve, it is crucial to invest in the development of platforms that can meet the changing needs of learners and educators, ensuring that all students have the opportunity to develop the skills they need to succeed in an increasingly digital world.

### 10.9 Integration of Metaverse and Immersive Technologies

The integration of metaverse and immersive technologies, such as virtual and augmented reality, into Scratch-based learning presents a compelling opportunity to transform computational thinking education. As the metaverse continues to evolve, it offers a new dimension for educational experiences that can significantly enhance student engagement, creativity, and problem-solving skills. By leveraging immersive technologies, educators can create more interactive and dynamic environments where students can explore computational concepts in a more intuitive and engaging way. This subsection explores the potential of integrating metaverse and immersive technologies with Scratch-based learning, highlighting how these innovations can support the development of computational thinking skills.

The metaverse, a collective virtual shared space created by the convergence of virtually enhanced physical and digital reality, offers an immersive environment for learning. In the context of computational thinking, the metaverse can provide students with opportunities to engage in collaborative projects, simulate real-world scenarios, and explore complex concepts in a more interactive manner. For instance, students can collaborate in virtual environments to design and develop projects that require computational thinking, such as creating interactive games or simulations. This not only enhances their understanding of computational concepts but also fosters creativity and collaboration. Research has shown that collaborative learning environments can significantly improve students' problem-solving skills and deepen their understanding of computational thinking [139].

Moreover, the integration of augmented reality (AR) and virtual reality (VR) into Scratch-based learning can provide students with hands-on experiences that simulate real-world applications of computational thinking. For example, AR can be used to overlay digital information onto the physical world, allowing students to interact with virtual objects in their environment. This can be particularly beneficial for teaching concepts such as data structures and algorithms, as students can visualize and manipulate abstract concepts in a more tangible way. Similarly, VR can create fully immersive environments where students can explore and experiment with computational thinking concepts in a safe and controlled setting. By engaging students in these immersive experiences, educators can enhance their understanding of computational thinking and foster a deeper connection to the subject matter.

The potential of integrating metaverse and immersive technologies with Scratch-based learning is further supported by the growing interest in using such technologies to enhance educational experiences. For example, the emergence of large language models (LLMs) has opened new possibilities for creating personalized and adaptive learning environments. These models can be used to provide real-time feedback and support to students as they engage with immersive technologies, helping them to navigate complex computational concepts more effectively. By leveraging the capabilities of LLMs, educators can create dynamic and responsive learning environments that adapt to the needs and preferences of individual students [129].

In addition to enhancing engagement and understanding, the integration of metaverse and immersive technologies can also support the development of computational thinking skills in diverse learning contexts. For instance, students with different learning styles and abilities can benefit from immersive experiences that cater to their unique needs. By providing multiple modes of interaction and representation, educators can create inclusive learning environments that support all students in developing their computational thinking skills. This is particularly important for students from underrepresented groups, who may face additional challenges in accessing and engaging with computational thinking education [107].

Furthermore, the integration of metaverse and immersive technologies can facilitate the development of interdisciplinary computational thinking skills. By combining computational thinking with other subjects such as science, mathematics, and the arts, educators can create more holistic and meaningful learning experiences. For example, students can use Scratch to create interactive projects that explore scientific concepts, mathematical problems, or artistic expressions. This interdisciplinary approach not only enhances students' understanding of computational thinking but also fosters creativity and critical thinking skills. Research has shown that interdisciplinary learning can significantly improve students' problem-solving abilities and deepen their understanding of complex concepts [2].

Another important aspect of integrating metaverse and immersive technologies with Scratch-based learning is the potential for personalized and adaptive learning experiences. By leveraging data analytics and machine learning, educators can create learning environments that adapt to the needs and preferences of individual students. For example, personalized learning paths can be created based on students' performance and progress, ensuring that they receive the appropriate level of challenge and support. This can help students to stay engaged and motivated, as they can see their progress and understand how they are developing their computational thinking skills. Moreover, the use of data analytics can provide educators with valuable insights into student learning, enabling them to make informed decisions about curriculum design and instructional strategies [140].

In conclusion, the integration of metaverse and immersive technologies with Scratch-based learning holds significant potential for transforming computational thinking education. By creating more engaging and interactive learning experiences, educators can enhance students' understanding of computational concepts, foster creativity and collaboration, and support the development of interdisciplinary skills. The use of immersive technologies can also facilitate personalized and adaptive learning environments, ensuring that all students have the opportunity to develop their computational thinking skills. As the metaverse continues to evolve, it is essential for educators to explore and leverage these technologies to create innovative and effective learning experiences that prepare students for the challenges of the digital age. The future of computational thinking education lies in the integration of these technologies, which can help to make learning more accessible, engaging, and meaningful for all students.

### 10.10 Advanced Data-Driven Computational Thinking Assessment

Advanced data-driven computational thinking (CT) assessment represents a promising direction in the field of educational technology, particularly in the context of Scratch-based learning. As computational thinking becomes an essential skill for students of all ages, the need for effective assessment tools that can accurately measure and enhance these skills has never been more critical. Data-driven approaches, including learning analytics and artificial intelligence (AI), offer innovative ways to assess and improve CT skills developed through Scratch. These methods leverage the vast amounts of data generated during the learning process, enabling educators to gain deeper insights into student learning and provide more personalized support.

One of the most significant advantages of data-driven assessment is its ability to provide real-time feedback and insights. By analyzing the data generated from student interactions with Scratch, educators can identify areas where students may be struggling and intervene accordingly. For instance, the use of automated testing in Scratch-based learning environments has been shown to be effective in generating next-step hints that help students overcome specific challenges. In a study by Catnip, it was found that automated testing can be used to select suitable candidate solutions for hint generation, resulting in more individualized and effective feedback for students [141]. This approach not only helps students improve their problem-solving skills but also fosters a more engaging and interactive learning experience.

Another area where data-driven approaches have shown promise is in the use of learning analytics to track student progress and identify patterns in learning behaviors. Learning analytics involves the collection and analysis of data to understand how students learn and how they can be supported. For example, in the study "Enhancing STEM Learning with ChatGPT and Bing Chat as Objects to Think With: A Case Study," it was found that conversational agents like ChatGPT and Bing Chat can be used as "objects-to-think-with," resources that foster reflective and critical thinking, and concept comprehension in enhancing STEM education [108]. This suggests that similar data-driven approaches can be applied to CT assessment, allowing educators to monitor student progress and provide targeted support based on individual learning needs.

The integration of AI into computational thinking assessment also holds great potential. AI can be used to analyze student work, identify patterns, and provide personalized recommendations for improvement. For instance, in the study "The AI Revolution in Education: Will AI Replace or Assist Teachers in Higher Education," it was discussed how AI can be used to enhance teaching and learning without replacing human teachers [142]. This aligns with the idea that AI can be used to support CT assessment by providing insights into student performance and offering suggestions for improvement. By leveraging AI, educators can create a more personalized and effective learning experience for students.

Moreover, data-driven assessment can help in identifying and addressing learning gaps. By analyzing the data generated from student interactions with Scratch, educators can identify common misconceptions and areas where students may be struggling. This information can then be used to develop targeted interventions and provide additional support. For example, in the study "Computational Thinking in Education: Where does it Fit: A systematic literary review," it was emphasized that there is a need for more detailed lesson plans and curriculum structures to support the teaching of CT [2]. Data-driven approaches can help in this regard by providing the necessary insights to inform curriculum development and instructional strategies.

The use of data-driven assessment also has implications for the development of new educational tools and technologies. For instance, in the study "Scratch Community Blocks: Supporting Children as Data Scientists," it was highlighted that tools like Scratch Community Blocks can enable children to programmatically access, analyze, and visualize data about their participation in Scratch [10]. This suggests that similar tools can be developed to support CT assessment, allowing students to engage with data and develop their analytical skills. By integrating data-driven assessment into educational tools, educators can create a more dynamic and interactive learning environment.

Furthermore, data-driven assessment can contribute to the development of more effective educational policies and practices. By analyzing data from a large number of students, educators and policymakers can identify trends and patterns that can inform the development of educational strategies. For example, in the study "The Impact of Artificial Intelligence on the Evolution of Digital Education: A Comparative Study of OpenAI Text Generation Tools including ChatGPT, Bing Chat, Bard, and Ernie," it was emphasized that the integration of AI in education has the potential to transform teaching methodologies and curriculum planning [91]. This suggests that data-driven approaches can play a crucial role in shaping the future of CT education by providing the necessary insights to inform policy decisions.

In addition, data-driven assessment can help in the development of more effective assessment methods. Traditional assessment methods often rely on standardized tests, which may not accurately reflect the complexity of CT skills. Data-driven approaches, on the other hand, can provide a more comprehensive and nuanced understanding of student learning. For instance, in the study "Personalization, Cognition, and Gamification-based Programming Language Learning: A State-of-the-Art Systematic Literature Review," it was found that personalized gamification can enhance student cognition in programming courses by improving motivation, engagement, and learning outcomes [131]. This suggests that similar data-driven approaches can be applied to CT assessment, allowing educators to develop more effective and engaging assessment methods.

In conclusion, the use of data-driven approaches, including learning analytics and AI, in computational thinking assessment offers a range of benefits. These approaches can provide real-time feedback, identify learning gaps, support the development of new educational tools, inform policy decisions, and enhance assessment methods. By leveraging the data generated from student interactions with Scratch, educators can gain deeper insights into student learning and provide more personalized support. As the field of educational technology continues to evolve, the integration of data-driven assessment into CT education will play a crucial role in shaping the future of learning and teaching.

### 10.11 Long-Term Impacts and Lifelong Learning

The long-term impacts of Scratch-based computational thinking education on students' learning trajectories and its contribution to lifelong learning and digital literacy are significant and multifaceted. Scratch, as a visual programming environment, introduces students to the foundational concepts of computational thinking (CT) in a way that is accessible, engaging, and adaptable to different learning contexts. Over time, these early experiences with CT can shape students' cognitive development, foster problem-solving skills, and encourage a deeper understanding of digital systems, all of which are essential for lifelong learning and digital literacy.

One of the most notable long-term benefits of Scratch-based CT education is the development of problem-solving skills that extend beyond the classroom. Research has shown that students who engage with Scratch are not only able to understand and apply fundamental CT concepts like decomposition, pattern recognition, and algorithmic thinking, but they also develop a mindset that emphasizes systematic and logical reasoning [2]. These skills are not confined to programming; they are transferable to various domains, including mathematics, science, and even everyday decision-making. By fostering a structured approach to problem-solving, Scratch-based learning lays the groundwork for students to tackle complex challenges throughout their academic and professional lives.

Moreover, the integration of creativity and expression through Scratch plays a crucial role in long-term learning outcomes. Students are encouraged to design their own projects, such as games, animations, and interactive stories, which not only reinforces CT concepts but also cultivates a sense of ownership and personal investment in their learning. This creative aspect of Scratch aligns with the principles of constructivism, where learners actively construct knowledge through meaningful and engaging experiences [2]. As students progress through different educational stages, the ability to think creatively and apply CT principles in novel contexts becomes a valuable asset, contributing to their overall adaptability and innovation in an increasingly digital world.

The influence of Scratch on lifelong learning is further underscored by its role in promoting digital literacy. As technology continues to permeate every aspect of modern life, the ability to understand, navigate, and critically engage with digital tools is no longer optional—it is essential. Scratch-based CT education provides students with a foundational understanding of how digital systems work, from the basics of programming to the logical structures that underpin software development. This knowledge empowers students to become more informed and capable users of technology, enabling them to participate effectively in a digital society [2].

Additionally, the long-term impacts of Scratch-based CT education can be observed in the development of a growth mindset and resilience. The iterative nature of programming, where students continuously test, debug, and refine their projects, teaches them to approach challenges with persistence and a willingness to learn from failure. This iterative process mirrors the real-world problem-solving strategies used in various professional fields, reinforcing the idea that learning is an ongoing and dynamic process. Research on the relationship between CT and critical thinking has highlighted the importance of this mindset in fostering a lifelong commitment to learning [3]. Students who develop this mindset are more likely to seek out new challenges, embrace continuous learning, and adapt to changing technological landscapes.

The impact of Scratch-based CT education on lifelong learning is also evident in the way it supports interdisciplinary learning. CT is not confined to computer science; it intersects with multiple disciplines, including mathematics, science, and the arts. By encouraging students to apply CT principles in diverse contexts, Scratch-based learning helps them recognize the relevance of computational thinking across different areas of knowledge. This interdisciplinary approach not only enhances students' understanding of CT but also prepares them to think critically and creatively about complex, real-world problems [2].

Another critical aspect of the long-term impacts of Scratch-based CT education is its potential to bridge the digital divide and promote equity in education. Scratch is freely accessible and can be used in a variety of settings, including schools, after-school programs, and informal learning environments. This accessibility ensures that students from diverse backgrounds have the opportunity to engage with CT and develop the digital skills necessary for future success. Studies on the use of Scratch in special needs and inclusive education have demonstrated its effectiveness in supporting diverse learners, including those with cognitive, motor, or sensory challenges [37]. By providing an inclusive platform for CT education, Scratch contributes to a more equitable distribution of digital literacy and computational skills.

In conclusion, the long-term impacts of Scratch-based computational thinking education on students' learning trajectories are profound. It not only enhances problem-solving and critical thinking skills but also fosters creativity, resilience, and a growth mindset. By promoting digital literacy and interdisciplinary learning, Scratch-based CT education prepares students for lifelong learning in a rapidly evolving digital world. The accessibility and adaptability of Scratch ensure that these benefits are available to a wide range of learners, making it a powerful tool for promoting equity and inclusion in education. As the field of CT education continues to evolve, the role of Scratch in shaping the next generation of digital thinkers and problem solvers will remain a vital component of this ongoing journey [2].

## 11 Conclusion

### 11.1 Recap of Key Findings

The comprehensive survey on Computational Thinking (CT) in Education with Scratch has revealed several major insights and findings that highlight the crucial role of Scratch in the development of computational thinking skills among learners of all ages. Scratch, as a block-based programming environment, has proven to be an effective tool for fostering CT by making programming accessible, engaging, and relevant to diverse educational contexts. The survey underscores that Scratch's visual and interactive nature not only simplifies the learning process but also enhances students' problem-solving abilities, logical reasoning, and creativity.

One of the key findings is that Scratch plays a significant role in the early development of computational thinking. Studies have shown that introducing CT through Scratch at an early age can significantly improve students' understanding of abstract concepts such as decomposition, pattern recognition, abstraction, and algorithm design [2]. For instance, research has demonstrated that Scratch enables students to break down complex problems into manageable parts and design solutions in an interactive environment, thus fostering a deeper understanding of computational concepts. This aligns with the theoretical foundations of CT, which emphasize the importance of these foundational skills in problem-solving processes [143].

Another important insight is that Scratch promotes creativity and self-expression by allowing students to create games, animations, and interactive stories. This not only engages students but also supports diverse learning styles, making the learning experience more personalized and meaningful [61]. The survey highlights that the creative aspect of Scratch encourages students to think outside the box and explore multiple solutions to a problem, which is a critical component of computational thinking [3]. Moreover, empirical studies have shown that Scratch-based learning can significantly enhance students' creativity and innovation, as evidenced by the development of unique projects and the use of advanced features such as DSAScratch and CryptoScratch [37].

The survey also emphasizes the role of collaborative learning in Scratch-based environments. Research indicates that Scratch facilitates collaborative learning by enabling students to work together on programming tasks, share projects, and engage in peer reviews [61]. This collaborative approach not only enhances students' social interaction but also supports the development of teamwork and communication skills. Furthermore, studies have shown that collaborative projects in Scratch can improve students' problem-solving abilities and deepen their understanding of computational concepts through shared learning experiences [68].

Another significant finding is that Scratch has a positive impact on student engagement and learning outcomes. Research has consistently shown that students who use Scratch are more engaged in the learning process and demonstrate improved computational thinking skills, problem-solving abilities, and motivation [14]. This is particularly evident in K-12 education, where Scratch has been successfully integrated into the curriculum to teach programming, problem-solving, and creativity through engaging and interactive projects [37]. Moreover, empirical studies have demonstrated that Scratch-based learning can lead to long-term improvements in students' computational thinking skills, as evidenced by their ability to apply these skills in real-world contexts [37].

The survey also highlights the challenges and limitations of using Scratch for computational thinking education. While Scratch is highly effective in introducing CT to young learners, there are challenges related to transitioning from block-based to text-based programming and overcoming cognitive barriers [61]. Additionally, the survey notes that there are limitations in teaching advanced computational thinking concepts with Scratch, such as data structures, algorithms, and abstraction, which may require more advanced programming environments [78]. However, the integration of emerging technologies such as AI and gamification into Scratch-based learning has shown promise in addressing some of these challenges and enhancing the learning experience [109].

The survey also underscores the importance of teacher training and support in effectively utilizing Scratch in the classroom. Research has shown that educators often face challenges in integrating Scratch into their teaching due to a lack of proper training and insufficient understanding of computational thinking concepts [41]. Therefore, the development of teacher training programs and curriculum resources is essential to ensure that Scratch is effectively utilized in educational settings [37]. Additionally, the survey highlights the need for ongoing professional development and support for educators to keep up with the evolving landscape of computational thinking education [106].

In conclusion, the survey on Computational Thinking in Education with Scratch has demonstrated that Scratch is a powerful tool for fostering computational thinking skills among learners of all ages. The visual and interactive nature of Scratch makes programming accessible and engaging, while its support for creativity, collaboration, and problem-solving enhances the learning experience. Despite the challenges and limitations, the integration of emerging technologies and the development of teacher training programs can further enhance the effectiveness of Scratch in computational thinking education. The findings of this survey provide valuable insights for educators, researchers, and policymakers in advancing the use of Scratch in the classroom and promoting computational thinking as a fundamental skill for the 21st century.

### 11.2 The Role of Scratch in Computational Thinking Education

Scratch has emerged as a pivotal tool in the landscape of computational thinking (CT) education, offering a unique blend of accessibility, engagement, and effectiveness for learners of all ages. Its visual, block-based interface removes the barriers of traditional text-based programming, allowing learners to focus on logical reasoning, algorithmic thinking, and problem-solving without the cognitive load of syntax. This makes Scratch an ideal platform for introducing computational thinking to young learners, as well as for reinforcing these skills in more advanced educational contexts. The role of Scratch in CT education is not only foundational but also transformative, as it bridges the gap between abstract computational concepts and tangible, creative outcomes.

One of the most significant contributions of Scratch to CT education is its ability to make programming accessible to a wide range of learners. Unlike traditional programming languages, which require a deep understanding of syntax and structure, Scratch uses a drag-and-drop interface that simplifies the process of coding. This feature is particularly beneficial for younger students, who may not yet have the fine motor skills or reading proficiency to engage with text-based programming languages. By focusing on visual blocks that represent programming concepts, Scratch enables learners to grasp the fundamentals of CT without being overwhelmed by the technicalities of code.
